RATING: 3
Two things stand out; it's an engine builder and it's (somewhat) asymmetrical in it's strategy. The later (in particular) salvages the game IMHO as it prevents players from just cracking the engine formulas and running the game into the ground. Still possible, but less likely. Driven by cards, each side will ultimately build a slightly different engine that is tailored to their faction's strength and to counter the other faction. In particular, that what the opponents faction is helps (in some way) determine what you should do to counter them; "do they produce lots of gold by require workers? Time to raze their worker production and hamper them." That sort of stuff help gives the game some staying power as each faction will have a weakness and counter that you will need to work on. The cards really save it as it's not a rote execution of each factions strategy; the initial lookout draft and what cards comes up also keeps the game fresh. The solo game is no slouch, and if you're into that sort of thing works really well, but the game shines at 2p. Above that, and I find it drags and it's tough to keep track of what everyone else is doing (although, that's only necessary when razing as there is only so much one can do to stop people outside of that).

I think the three dings I have against it are that it's basically an engine builder (which I find aren't particularly interesting or interactive). It solves the later by asymmetry and ability to break sections of their engine which is nice. Second, while it's interactive in the draft (passively) and razing (actively), that's about it. I get the appeal, it's just not something that turns me on. Last, passing sort of blows (although the exploration tile promos *do* help solve that problem). Still, an ok game and I do enjoy my plays (even the solo mode was interesting and deserving of praise), but it only gets so many plays before it just gets old, for instance, Lewis & Clark has a much more interesting and opaque decision tree, which holds it together, but L&C less interactive which is a trade off.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
04-27-2015: After two plays, this could go up to an 8 or further down to a 6 or 5 based on how the next play goes. I've played three of Ignacy's games now (this along with Witcher and Robinson Crusoe) and the overwhelming feeling I've had through three different games and 7-8 hours of combined gameplay is: frustration. They haven't been fun, which to me is a cardinal sin for a board game.

I think this one would probably be best capped at 1-2 players. Restricting it to one action per player per turn makes the game exponentially longer. I need to play a card, so I do an action that gives me resources. Wait. Wait. Wait. Spend those resources to play the card. Wait. Wait. Wait. Use an action to get people. Wait. Wait. Wait. Play people on the card for points. Sigh... this makes me really appreciate games like Manhattan Project where I can do a similar series of turns in about 30 seconds.

It's very much a multiplayer solitaire. Unless someone is killing your cards you don't really care what anyone else is doing. The 45-90 minutes suggested game length is laughable. Our two games so far with three and four players have taken 2-3 hours each. You only go through a portion of your deck so it's very luck dependent on what cards you happen to draw. And hopefully if you were lucky enough to draw a good card you got it earlier in the game rather than later.

I feel this game would be really great if you could cut down on the game length and make the card draws more strategic versus luck-based (ability to look through your deck rather than shuffle and hope). This should also be 90 minutes at most but it's never been close to that for us.

Disclaimer: I usually play as the color red, so both games so far I played with Romans. If there is a combination of cards I should play, I haven't found them yet.

UPDATE 05-05-15: I played it again as a 2 player game with my wife and enjoyed it a lot more that way - significantly less downtime - and played as the Barbarians rather than the Romans.

UPDATE 06-06-15: I played again as the Japanese and won handily, after finishing in last place my first three games. I don't see my rating going up much at all from here. I feel about this game similar to the way I feel about Seasons -- great components, great cards, but in order to win I have to be a jerk, and I hate those types of games.

RATING: 6
This game is a huge disappointment for me because it has the potential to be amazing. Great components, nice engine-building mechanics, elegant simple-to-grasp rules.. there is so much to like. Oh, and I wanted to love this game, but by the end of the second round of my 3-player game, I already knew the winner of the game - and it was all luck of the draw. We watched as that initial great draw was then compounded... and  suddenly, a player who rarely ever wins our games came away with a runaway victory.  We razed, but as he was playing barbarians, most of his buildings were faction buildings. Another player attacked to disable a building, with the one such card he drew. I drew no offensive cards, but I did make an effort to draft a card that would have increased his lead. And, despite playing Romans, I only drew a couple of production cards. Sadly, due to the way the game is designed, there is both a firm limit on draw opportunities (...we managed not to have a single card in the draft that gave those until the very last round) and very little opportunity to shift strategy. Certain decks demand certain play strategies and if you can't draw the cards, it's a disaster. Still, it was an enjoyable experience and I'm glad that player got a chance to win a game, but I can't see myself choosing to play this over engine-builders with better balance: Viticulture (with Tuscany), Roll for the Galaxy, Scythe... and so forth. I may reconsider if an expansion that fixes this issue comes out.

RATING: 7.5
Font size and iconography are incredibly small and this immediately makes the game look a little amateurish, which is a shame because it's a fairly complex design. That's not to say that it's complex to play - the rules are extremely intuitive. There is a lot of attacking (with a variant ruleset to reduce this, which I think will be ignored by most players). The negative interaction is a downside for me, but I'm sure others will love it. This is straight-up resource-gathering/engine-building and there is nothing particularly innovative here, but it is done well.

RATING: 5
1 play / 4 players

LIKE: Lots of powers and combos to explore. Fun to plan out your production cycle. Cards graphics are well done.
DISLIKE: Small text on cards, bleh! High conflict (eye-for-an-eye attacks) is a big minus, especially for the Japanese faction. Some downtime for the players who passed in the round.

OVERALL: Felt a little bit like Nations, but I enjoy the player interaction in Nations better. In Imperial Settlers, I felt the razing to be too confrontational, "eye for an eye", which I cannot stand. Without that conflict, it is too hard to slow down leaders, making some combo engines ominously powerful. Not bad, but I'd rather play something else. Maybe a couple more plays would help.

RATING: 7
The funniest part of the game is to figure out new card combos. And best part is that only a limited amount is available to you at the same time. Thus the option space is not infinitely large making the game relatively quick playing. Building your empire and seeing it grow is nice and every round there are a number of tough decisions, too.  All this makes the game quite satisfying.

However, despite of occasional razing and a few drafted cards the game lacks player interaction. Basically the game is a race for points, and in all races you should have a way to block other players. In Imperial Settlers you have none. Ok, you can raze but that is a reaction rather than proactive course of action. In other words, Imperial Settlers is a bit of a solitaire.

Sometimes you raise buildings, sometimes you make deals with other cultures, sometimes you exploit your civilizations synergies to achieve good outcomes and depreciate your opponents. It all ends up looking like a bunch of Care Bears trying to murder each other with butcher knives. Fun times.

RATING: 8
I love this game!  Engine-building at its finest!

My rating reflects two competing thoughts/ratings:  I give this a 9 for solo play and a 6-7 for multiplayer (using the rules as written).

Solo Play:  The given solo rules are solid and the official campaign variant pushes it over the top.  Very well done!

Multiplayer:  Good but some individual faction buildings needlessly push at the inherent balance in poor ways.  They enable unusual, destructive measures that don't need to be there.  It's enough that players can raze common buildings (barring the Japanese) - there's no need to upset that by allowing certain factions, having drawn and built certain buildings, to totally ignore that fundamental basis/balance.  I would like multiplayer a LOT more with the removal of some of those uber-destructive buildings.

Overall an excellent game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Wow, do I love this game! I like it so much, that I'm ashamed I let it flew under my radar for so long. Maybe my tastes changed over the years, maybe I first needed to see it whether this game had any staying power or expansions... but, after finally playing it, I became addicted to it! I stared playing it on a regular basis, I bought all the expansion and it just so happened that Imperial Settlers became my favorite game of all time!

Imperial Settlers is a beautiful game. It has great wooden components, incredible artwork and a very cool theme, that I very much prefer to the post-apocalyptic one of its older brother. What I find the most exciting about it though is its strategical depth/ease of play ratio. You can explain the game pretty quickly, but beneath a bunch of wonderfully crafted mechanisms lies a game with a lot of choices to offer. The key element of this depth is, of course, the multi-use cards. Every hand in Imperial Settlers is a puzzle. Depending on your strategy, your faction or you specific short-term goals you need to figure out how put those cards at the best use. It might be difficult, you might do mistakes, but it's so damn satisfying when you do it right!

Yet this game has a whole lot more to offer than this! It has in fact nearly the perfect level of player interaction for me. In many video I've seen reviewers complaining either for the excessive meanness of the game or, at the other end of the spectrum, for its lack of player interaction. The reason for this opposite views is that the interaction really depends on you, your faction and on your playing style... and personally I find this to be really great, because I can adapt it to the taste of the other players at the table or to my particular mood that day. Yet the one thing that makes this game reach the next level is the factions! They are not just a player board or a card with a special power... they are a completely unique deck of cards, a totally different style of play. Each faction plays almost like a different game and this makes Imperial Settlers incredibly re-playable!

There, however, are a few things that might be bothersome to somebody. There's some luck of the draw, even if I personally think that it's not so relevant to invalidate the cool elements of the game. There's also a steep learning curve that might scare people away or even make them leave the table with the feeling that a particular faction is over-powered. That's a complaint I've heard a lot already, but I really disagree with it. I believe in fact that, when people think a faction is overpowered, more often than not it's because that one faction was being played by someone who knew the game better.

Criticisms aside, all in all I think this an amazing game and one that, every time I play it, leaves me with the desire of coming back to it soon... and all the different expansions that came out only made it better. Imperial Settlers has now an incredible amount of variability, that keeps bringing me back to it. I like the new factions a lot, the Atlanteans being my favorite, and the mechanisms that they introduce. I like to deck-build and explore new strategies and combos. There's just so much to see in this game! It's definitively my favorite of all time. It's incredible! I love it!

Rating without expansions: 8.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is one of the best card based civilization games until now. You have four rounds, which you start by drafting cards and then by acquiring the available resource you effectively lay them down in an orderly fashion to build up your county (Japan, Rome, Egypt or the Barbarians). You then may use if able the special abilities provided by these cards to buy more cards and lay down even more of them. Also there is a neat combat system in which you are able to destroy your opponents buildings or even to raize your own and collect some resources (wood) in case of shortage. The mechanics work well, althought the available four civilizations doesn't provide for much gameplay variability.

RATING: 6
1st play: Quite a cool game, different race powers, shared pool of cards, drafting some buildings and getting an engine going. The art is appealing and the mechanics are interesting. The game could really use a solid player aid to help learning. Also, first plays can be swingy, more plays may help mitigate this, or it might come down to card draw.

2nd play: I played a different race this time and figuring out how to combo the cards of the race was fun.  Avoiding the pitfalls and trying to build an engine was cool, but it was a little frustrating working to put it all together only to have a critical building killed at the wrong time.  Two of the people at the table felt like their powers were very boring, and that level of disparity between races ends up making the game not as fun for me.  I am interested to try those factions, but I have a feeling they're right about those factions.

RATING: 9
[b]After 6 plays[/b]:
Imperial Settlers is a game that I found out about first by the commotion it produced in our local community when it was announced (many people pre-ordered it). After that, I heard that it somewhat takes after the "old" PC game The Settlers, I game which I liked and played while discovering videogames. This last thing made me pull the trigger and buy it. Oh, what a good choice!

I like card games (RFtG is my all time favourite), but I would love to play them less and more boardgames. This game scratches two itches: while building a tableau, you must create an economic engine and generate the resources needed for it.

There are so many pros: the wooden components (resource-meeples), the many ways you can use a card, the different strategies, based on the which faction you play.

Now to talk about the dark side: the downtime and the future of the game. I have played with a maximum of 3 players (just one of us was new to the game) till now, but it took too long for me to ever try it with 4 players (unless they are experienced players). Regarding the future of the game, it looks that it is heading towards a TCG/LCG path. I hate deckbuilding and if that will turn out to be true, I will stick with just the base game.

This is the best new game I have played in 2014, no doubt about it. The most interesting part is that my wife has won just one of the 5 games with have played till now and she still wants to play it.

RATING: 8.2
A surprisingly fun medium-weight game, in spite of the non-existent theme! I like it a lot more than Race for the Galaxy, with which it's often compared. Card-game fans may be disappointed if they play with a strategy of, "I'll do this once such-and-such a card comes up," because there's no guarantee that the card you want will show up! One's strategy must be balanced with a strong sense of the tactical.

The art is OK...if you can actually see it! My goodness, the art, icons and text are awfully small on the cards. Imperial Settlers would also benefit from player aids. The only other complaint I have is the game length of five rounds: seven would have been a lot better. At the end of Round Five, you're thinking, "Hey! I was just getting started!"

RATING: 8
Excellent game, and by far the most accessible in the series (51st -> New Era -> Imperial Settlers).  Open-ended freeform asymetric tableau/engine builder where you try and score the most points in five rounds, so simple for people to pick up and conceptualize.  Surprisingly tricky learning curve, and lots of tough decisions to make.  More player interaction than one would expect, and can get somewhat spiteful depending on which cards come out.  Table talk is important.

RATING: 6
Trzewiczek's most accessible and streamlined attempt at a tableau builder.  Cards are multi-purpose, which mitigates the luck of the draw.  Lack the resources to build that one, then you can raze it to gain the resources necessary to build something else.  Also, with some of the cards you can make a deal to increase production of one resource. In the time honoured fashion, the decisions in this game are about how best to use your cards in hand and on the table to develop a resource (and then victory point) engine.  

Unlike most engine builders, Imperial Settlers has an element of direct interaction in that it is possible to raze opponents' buildings to gain resources and damage their engines. The extent to which razing is used to gather the resources you need or to bash whoever you perceive to be the leader is an interesting judgement. 

There are four factions in the box, each with its own deck.  They certainly play quite differently. A question in my mind is about how strongly the cards of a faction steer your play in one direction or another.    

I have quite enjoyed playing Imperial Settlers.  However, while it all works smoothly, there is a sense of deja vu about this game.  It lacks something fresh to make it really stand out.

The computer civ game look of the artwork is quite jolly.  However, the tiny text on the cards was a poor design choice.  This is an interactive game and I need to know what the cards in your tableau can do.

RATING: 7.5
Δυνατό light-civ καρτοπαίχνιδο. Εννοώ light αν το κρίνουμε σαν civilization παιχνίδι αλλά γεμάτο σαν καρτοπαίχνιδο. Κάθε παίκτης έχει την δικά του τράπουλα με κτήρια της φυλής του αλλά υπάρχει και μια κοινή τράπουλα για όλους. Οπότε οι παίκτες μαζεύουν resources και χτίζουν διάφορα κτήρια, που με την σειρά τους παράγουν περισσότερα resources ενώ προσφέρουν και ικανότητες. Όποιος φτιάξει την καλύτερη μηχανή παραγωγής VP κερδίζει. Κλασσική δοκιμασμένη συνταγή.

Το artwork είναι πολύ συμπαθητικό όπως και γενικά η ποιότητα κατασκευής. Θα ήθελα απλά λίγο μεγαλύτερη γραμματοσειρά στις κάρτες γιατί δεν μπορούσα να διαβάσω τις κάρτες των αντιπάλων. Οι 4 φυλές (Ρωμαίοι, Αιγύπτιοι, Ιάπωνες και Βάρβαροι) είναι αρκετά διαφορετικές μεταξύ τους.

Οι κανόνες είναι απλοί και το παιχνίδι κυλά γρήγορα αλλά παρόλα αυτά τον θέλει τον χρόνο του. Πολύ πιθανό οι πρώτες παρτίδες να ξεπεράσουν το 2ωρο για 4 παίκτες. Να σημειώσω εδώ πως η αλληλεπίδραση μεταξύ των παικτών είναι μικρή παρόλο που μπορείς να επιτεθείς σε κάποια από τα κτήρια των αντιπάλων. Άρα μάλλον είναι προτιμότερο να παιχθεί με 2 άντε 3 άτομα. Ο τέταρτος δεν προσφέρει κάτι εκτός από έξτρα downtime.

Πάντα μου αρέσουν τέτοιου είδους παιχνίδια με κάρτες. Ανυπομονώ να δω τι θα προσθέσουν τα expansions. Υπάρχει χώρος για βελτίωση.

A favor.

- Reglas muy asequibles que se explican en 10 minutos.

- El juego es entretenido, mucho combo de cartas y gestión de recursos.

- Sin ser de civilizaciones tiene un aire de evolución.

- El arte gráfico es muy divertido.

En contra.

- Con el transcurrir de las partidas puede llegar a ser bastante repetitivo.

- Muy poca interacción. No existe apenas forma de bloquear de algún modo los progresos de otro jugador. Sin duda es un solitario multijugador.

- No lo veo a más de dos jugadores. Es costoso controlar lo que uno tiene en mesa, como para estar pendiente de los efectos de las cartas que tienen bajadas los demás.

- Muy dependiente del orden en el que vas robando las cartas de tu mazo de civilización. Si no entran los combos buenos te descuelgas de la puntuación y es imposible remontar.

- Las expansiones no me acaban de convencer. Serían de agradecer nuevas civilizaciones más originales.

RATING: 7
A wonderful card-driven engine building game that is built around a very simple rule set. When I first got the game, I was a bit skeptical for two reasons. First, as a euro-gamer, I was concerned about the ability to attack other players and how that element may introduce meta-gaming. But in practice, attacking other players is costly, gives them a resource (wood), and may be inconsequential depending the card. The second element that concerned me was luck. But because of how cards are handled (You do not lose them... unlike other resources) and how they can combo together, players can simply hold onto the cards until they are able to better utilize them. In this way, a bad starting hand is much less significant. 

As I have played the game more, the one thing I've noticed is that the game can really slow down with competent players. Essentially, the game is about drawing cards. As you draw more cards, you have more options and a greater ability to combo them together so you can play/use/draw-more cards. Thus, situations where players have 15-20+ cards and trying to plan out how they will play them isn't uncommon. But honestly... that's not a bad thing for me. The fact that such a simple game can create such an intricate puzzle only adds to the richness of the game. And turns are still relatively short, as players can only do 1-action per turn. But it does turn a 30-min card game... with some luck... into a 1.5-hour card game... with some luck. While luck is well controlled through how cards are utilize, it's still there and somewhat distasteful in a longer game.

But overall, this is a very clean design with interesting choices. Furthermore, each faction plays differently and only adds to the richness of the game. Combined that with amusing art-work... it's a winner. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I like variable use card games, but this doesn't quite work for me. I don't like the hard five round structure, which has no thematic justification and probably no mechanically one other than to artificially keep playing time within the box. I rather like games in which the players can trigger the end game condition, providing an extra layer of strategy.

Also, for a civilization building game, it does feel too stringy with the number of civilizations. Why can't I be the Greeks or Chinese or something? Any having a "barbarian" faction seem also theme breaking to me. Why can't they be the Mongols or Aztecs or something?

RATING: 7
I was charmed by my first play.  The art's whimsical style and 3/4 top down view is totally fantastic and reminds me a lot of the classic PC series "The Settlers" or maybe also "Travian".

Before this was released I told a friend that this game looked like something that I already own.  Civ-building, tableau-splaying, engine-churning card games just tickle my fancy I guess.

I haven't played it enough to know if it truly distinguishes itself from the likes of "7 Wonders", "San Juan / Race for the Galaxy" or the myriad other games where you can build a well, or a quarry, or a trading post.  

But the first play was fun!

***

2 more plays (one of which was a 4-player game).  I've gotten a few rules wrong so I need to play again.  I'm still quite taken with the overall presentation -- still not sure how it fits in with other tableau builders.

I do like how different the factions are.  I like that players need to evaluate their hand and consider the timing of their actions - taking into account the threat of attacks from other factions. 

Is it enough to keep this one around despite other strong competitors in the same space?  Jury is still out.

****

I need to play this again, but with Deus on the horizon, this one might hit the trading pile

****

Traded.  Despite a lot of potential ultimately it comes down to this:  Is it better than 7 wonders?  For me, the answer is "no"

RATING: 7
Nos nem rossz, de elsőre egy nagyon kicsit csalódás, talán túl nagy volt a várakozás:) De lesz még alkalma javítani, játszogatni fogunk vele most egy darabig. 

Nem tudom egyébként, miért vártam olyan sokat tőle, hisz az 51st state nekem egyáltalán nem tetszett. Olyan semmilyen volt, erőltetett, igaz hogy 1 partit játszottam csak vele, de az alapján nem győzött meg.

Itt viszont mindjárt adva van egy jobb téma (valódi történelmi népek, nem valami unalmas kamu sztori... 51. állam hagyjuk már). Na szóval a jól bevált népek nekem jobban bejönnek, és a grafika is jobb. Mivel 3 éve játszottam a másikkal, átolvastam a szabályát, hogy lássam a különbségeket. Hú tényleg jó nagy a hasonlóság, de azért nem ugyanaz a két szabály. Az 51 kicsit mélyebb, a Birodalmi viszont talán épp ezért letisztultabbnak, egzaktabbnak tűnik. Érdekes, hogy egy nagyon hasonló játékrendszer mennyire más játékot ad, ha a tálaláson változtatnak kicsit. Szóval a kezdő benyomások alapján jóra számítottam.

Mi az ami nem tetszett: először is nekünk a 3 fős parti 3 óra volt tisztán, rengeteg holtidővel. Oké tudom, első parti, de akkor is. Simán lehet, hogy valakinek dupla annyi akciója van, a másik akkor csak ül és nézi.
Emellett minden nemzet kártya pakliban van néhány nagyon erős lap. Nagyon sok múlik azon, hogy kinek (mikor) jön ki ilyen. Pl. a mi partinkban Egyiptom felépített egy a mienknél jobban működő kombót, és mivel nemzeti kártyáin volt, nem tudtunk vele mit kezdeni. Az utolsó 2 körben már látszott, hogy nem fogjuk tudni megfogni, nagy különbséggel fog nyerni. De Rómának és Japánnak is van olyan lapja, amivel eldobathattuk volna a kombójához fontos Szfinxeket, de ezek a lapok nem jöttek. Szóval kicsit nekem olyan a játék, hogy amolyan kőpapírolló jelleggel van mindenre ellenszer, kérdés, hogy ha az ellenfelemnél a kő van, akkor nálam épp a papír vagy az olló... És azért így ez elég szerencsefüggő. De nem akarom a szerencsére fogni hogy szarrávertek:) Egy kombóbajnok tuti azt mondaná, hogy leszarja mit csinál a másik, csinál nála egy jobb kombót. Én viszont eleve nem vagyok valami nagy kombókirály, másrészt ha egy ilyen jellegű játékot pasziánsszal folyamatosan nyerni lehet, az szerintem szar:) Nekem egyébként ez a játék sokkal pasziánszosabb, mint mondjuk a 7 csoda (azért ahhoz hasonlítom, mert olvastam kritikának, hogy az pasziánsz), ott nagyobb hatással vagyok az ellenfelekre, 3 főnél legalábbis.

Most szeretnénk végigpróbálgatni a népeket, mert hallottam olyat, hogy nem kiegyensúlyozottak, ennek utána kell járni:) A paklikat átnéztük, és szerintem mindegyikben benne van a nyerés, de néhány parti után térjünk erre vissza.

A sima rombolást egyébként nem érzem valami komoly interakciónak, általában a saját kézből éri meg rombolni, igazán fontos épületet ritkán tudtunk elcsípni az ellenfélnél. Néhány speckó lap több interakciót visz bele.

Mivel változatosak a paklik, sok jópofa épület van, szerintem sok játék lehet benne, csak ezen túl kell tenni magunkat, hogy a felhúzott lapokon bizony elég sok múlik. Tetszik az, hogy aszimmetrikus a játék, és minden nép egy kicsit más. 1-1 parti alapján nálam mindenképp ez a nyerő az 51-gyel szemben.

Egyébként nekem ez kb. olyan Dicsőség rómának szint, és a jellege is hasonló. A pontszám is egyelőre azonos:)
---
Na most hogy már próbáltam 1,2,3 fővel, kicsit letisztult néhány dolog: a játékidő kb. játékosonként 1 óra (lehet hogy a kieg miatt is, de szerintem nem sokat számít, hogy azzal játszottuk). Ezek alapján 4 fősre tuti nem vállalkoznék. A holtidő is nő a létszámmal, és sajnos többedik játékra sem sikerül leszorítani a játékidőt (ahogy jobbak leszünk, úgy lesz egyre több akciónk). Nekem ez kicsit elvette a kedvem, mert egy ilyen kategóriájú játékokból ne 1 férjen bele egy estébe. Dicsőség Rómának-ból legalább 2-3 lemegy egy este, sőt Egiziából vagy Kemetből is 2-3, annyira azért nem jó a Birodalmi, hogy ezeket 1 Birodalmira cseréljem. Sőt ez már a Goával, Power Griddel vagy Caylussal vetekszik időben, ami mind sokkal jobb játék (és még nagyon sok példát lehetne hozni). Összefoglalva: 1 órás kategóriában egész jónak számítana, de 1 óra/fős kategóriában már középszerű.

Az eddigiek alapján egyébként Egyiptomot érezzük a többinél kicsit erősebbnek. Érdekesség: eddig mindig a kezdő nyert (2 db 2 fős, 2 db 3 fős partiban).

Kipróbáltam a szólót is, nem rossz, de az egyiptomival nem okozott túl nagy kihívást maga a győzelem, talán azért is jött ki hozzá a nehezítő 2 kieg lap a virtuális játékosnak. A játék során egy közös lapot se hagytam kint a kör végére, azokat csak építési helynek használtam, így esélye se volt rombolni. Mondom, szerintem 1 parti alapján egyáltalán nem kihívás.

RATING: 7.5
Imperial Settlers is the descendant of 51st State, a tableau building card game I didn't fall in love with (probably because it fell short in the comparison with Race for the Galaxy, an all-time favorite).

Imperial Settlers has more streamlined rules (and _unbelievably_ a great rulebook! The first time for Ignacy...), and more different factions than 51st State, and seems more appealing even if it swapped a cute computer-like quasi-historical theme for an actual rich post apocalyptic setting.

Gameplay is intuitive, enjoyable and smooth, and would be almost great if the graphic design wasn't so totally screwed: icons and text are soooo tiny! And, in such a game of tableau building, where you should be able to see what other players actually have in play, this is a sizeable obstacle to playability...
This is a shame, because graphics are actually enjoyable when you look at the cards in your hand, but absolutely not readable at the other side of the table!

Anyway, I love this kind of tableau-building game. And Imperial Settlers is a very very good game in its genre!

RATING: 8.1
A very enjoyable and puzzley hand and resource management game with a flavor of civ building.  There is a small amount of multi use cards (faction cards can be played as "deals" for resources or to the tableaux for their abilities, common cards can only be played for their ability but can be razed (destroyed) for a one off award of resources and or points.  There are 4 different factions to play with and explore in the base game and now to extra factions available through expansions.  I like Bruges, San Juan and Race for the Galaxy much more and this feels a little similar to Bruges but different enough to have its own place in my collection and this is a great game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
:d10-2:

UPDATE: The rating took a big hit - going from 8 to 6, with the potential to go down to 4 - after my most recent play. There's just not enough interaction to warrant the incessant micro-actions. And the tensions in gameplay aren't as interesting as I had originally supposed.

OLD COMMENT: Imperial Settlers is the latest reimplementation of the 51st State game system. Some have called it watered-down, but in my opinion, the refinements obsolete earlier versions. Resource management is smoother, the asymmetrical powers are more interesting, and the mechanics are more intuitive.

My only real issue with the game (and it's a considerable one) is that there is very little direct interaction for the majority of the game, yet you still have to take a billion micro-actions, one at a time, just in case the order of player actions will matter. With experience, I'm hoping that this both speeds up and proves more relevant.

Also, there is enough 'take that' in here that I would not want to play this multiplayer (although my first game was, in fact, with four).

I'm definitely going to picking this one up.

RATING: 8
Lightly-themed civilization game, essentially hand management card game. It gives off a similar vibe to Terraforming Mars for me, steady engine builder as the number of actions you can perform vastly increased every round. The cutesy artwork makes the game more appealing to me and I like how all the factions have their own personal deck so you must play to your factions' strength. I think I'll like the game more once I'm more familiar with the cards! 

One thing I had to nitpick about is the font size, which can be easily enlarged since there's so much space on the cards. I think the game is actually best suited for 2-players, can play a game under an hour and still have the feeling of satisfaction of a heavy game.

RATING: 6
It's a very tight variable player power and economic game of min/maxing.  You start with a small income every turn and need to try to build it into something great through construction of public and personal buildings (cards) that provide actions/abilities.  In this regard, it's a tactical puzzle game in which you do the best with what luck of the draw give you; however, the payouts and trade-ups are small enough that it's a rather painful (blood from a stone) min/maxing efficiency puzzle, and it can be slow and plodding even with players that aren't prone to AP because the gains each turn are so small/incremental.  There's some interesting stuff going on, and I'd like to try it again, but I'm worried that the faction decks are too dissimilar (making it hard to teach newbies) and that the game is too long, considering the luck-of-the-draw and possible turn order advantage in the card draft each round.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5

I would recommend this to anyone who takes gaming seriously.

RATING: 8
Oh a wonderful low interaction tableu builder. Let's compare it to RftG:
 - slower
 - less freedom
 - more escalation
 - direct interaction via removal
 - more random
 - more tactical (order of actions is important!)
 - no role selection
 - better looking
 - more variety
 - better feeling of development
 - asymetric factions

Race has the more refined mechanics, but IS has a more epic and satisfying feel to it, when your action / production chains become very powerful towards the end of the game.

RATING: 5
I'm not quite sure how I feel about Imperial Settlers yet. I totally bought it based on hype and because I heard it was similar in some ways to one of my favorite games: Race for the Galaxy.

I like that you can use cards for multiple things and I like the producing and spending different resources (which is different than RftG). Having two decks (common and faction) adds some more tough decisions.

Caveats: I have not played enough to know the faction decks thoroughly (like I know what I could be fishing for in RftG) and I've only played the Barbarians and Romans.

Conclusion: So far I think I'd rather just play RftG or Archipelago? RftG plays quicker and has the benefit that I know all the cards while Archipelago does the whole resource collection and civ thing better (and not that much longer with only 2 people).

My wife really likes Imperial Settlers so that means at this point it's here to stay

I'm leaving myself space to say I was wrong once I play more and play the other factions :)

UPDATE: I traded it away because it just didn't see enough play. It was too slow for what it offered :/

RATING: 8.6
Rating including Why Can't We Be Friends, Atlanteans, and Aztecs
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I've played a bit of 51st State again recently, and I think the thing which makes the various iterations of that game superior is the "rule of 3". You can't just ride one killer combo to victory; good combination get played out and you have to always be looking for the next upgrade to you tableau. In Imperial Settlers, you *can* just ride that awesome combo you got on turn 1 or 2 for the win; each deck has a few killer combos in there and you just want to draw them fast.

Plus 51st State and New Era just have vastly better theme :)

---

As I play it more, Imperial Settlers is losing its grip on me a bit. I still like it and am happy to play it occasionally as I have friends who are fans, but the painfully bad usability throughout the game - tiny icons, ludicrous font sizes, even the readability on the fonts is poor - is just such a drag, especially since awareness of what's going on in other players' tableaux is fairly important. Also, each faction tends to revolve around a couple key buildings and combos, and players who just draw the combos early - there isn't much you can do to cycle cards if you don't get what you need - will be the ones who are in it with a chance to win. Like 51st State, the early turns are really key - if you can't draw a production building early, or some other bootstrap, you will be lacking options for the rest of the game and there just isn't much you can do about it. 51st State had a similar dynamic, but because it was a smaller game it seemed less painful there. Finally, Imperial Settlers is mostly a puzzle-solving game; you have a hand of cards with building costs and benefits and you need to figure out the right usage and order for each card to squeeze the most out of them. There just aren't enough useful combos in the card space (combined with too many relatively uninteresting cards) to really reward creative play.

Anyway, that sounds terribly harsh, and in fact is probably harsher than it needs to be. I'm probably more frustrated with Imperial Settlers by the fact that it's such a near miss, and by the stupid decisions on the presentation front. In fact I still find it enjoyable, it's a decent tableau/engine building game, but certainly vastly inferior to its cousin-games San Juan or Race for the Galaxy (those games are, of course, total classics). I even think it's a step backwards from 51st State. Sure, the icons in 51st State could be confusing, but at least you could *%^#ing see them.
---
I like Imperial Settlers but I find it oddly less compelling than 51st State and its follow-ons. Where 51st State had an actual interesting and imaginative setting and world, and gameplay that made some sense in that context, Imperial Settlers is just generic computer-gaming quasi-historical empire-building with actually quite minimal flavor and certainly no resemblance to any real-world empires. The sad thing is, Imperial Settlers' totally generic and pasted-on theme seems to be more appealing to people than 51st State's actually thoughtful world-building.

Better fonts, larger icons, and generally less amateurish presentation throughout and more flair in the faction design could easily earn this another ratings point.
---
*Very* similar to 51st State, although with better iconography and a very slightly streamlined system. If you liked/didn't like 51st State, it's hard to imagine you'll feel that much differently about Imperial Settlers.

Absolutely brutal fonts, though - significantly less readable than Dominion, and that's saying something. Text size is punishingly small, even some the icons are hard to distinguish at anything further away than in your hand. Absolutely unbelievable, actually. Portal is not a new kid on the block, they've been around a while, they should know better than this sort of idiocy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I love engine building games, and I also love games where you get what you get and make the best of it, so Imperial Settlers is right up my alley!

There does seem to be some potential for a runaway leader (we had a game where Egypt just trounced everyone with nothing anyone else could do about it), but I'm hoping that's the exception rather than the norm because I love everything about this game otherwise.

The multi-use cards are great and really make you think about the best way to play any given card, and the player interaction keeps you on the edge of your seat, hoping no one messes with your plans.

I really look forward to playing more, and also seeing what great new factions will be released in the future!

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play with 2 players as the Romans:

This is a nice little engine building/tableau game. It's hard to say based on one play whether or not it really outclasses any of the many existing games in its genre, but I would happily play it again.

The asymmetric element is the most unique part of the game but also what makes it so hard to judge after just one play. These types of games only tend to shine once you're familiar with the possible combos, and I'm definitely not there yet. With an asymmetric game, it's even harder as you want to have a good idea of what your opponents are capable of too.

I initially shied away from this game because of the "take that!" element (love direct conflict in games, but I find "take that!" stuff to be a poor way to go about it 99% of the time) but it actually handles it pretty well. The Razing mechanic works for two reasons: (1) the whole game is setup such that you're constantly tearing down your common locations to build buffer faction ones. So your enemies aren't tearing down something you were going to rely on for the whole game; they're merely doing something sooner than you intended forcing you to adjust your strategy for a different pace and (2) timing is EVERYTHING in this game.

Because you have a lot of flexibility around when you take different actions, strategizing purely around how you time your actions is absolutely key in this game. You're always optimizing your future turns in your head, and if you're smart, you can sometimes use this to avoid being razed at inopportune times. So while it's hard to be truly defensive, you do feel some sense of agency around when you get hit.

This game is quite a bit more fiddly than something like San Juan or Race (well, now Roll) for the Galaxy. There's an awful lot of just grabbing resource bits, shuffling them around, cleaning them up - it feels a bit much for such a quick, light-ish game. Given my preference for elegance I would be surprised if I ended up liking this game as much as those but for now it's really too early to say.

RATING: 7
Rated 8 after two back-to-back plays. I was hoping this game would deliver a fairly meaty card tableau type game, and so far, so good. My game group was interested, so I ended up playing this twice... once as barbarians and once as Romans. 

This is a slick running engine, with clearly defined actions, straightforward rounds (draft cards, gather resources, take actions... next round!) and a reasonable playing time. It strikes me that this might run so smoothly because it's essentially a 2.0 version of 51st State (which I've not played).

Everyone that played it enjoyed it, I think, and I look forward to trying the Egyptians and Japanese.

Update: Ultimately, I traded this one away. Each play I found it a little less interesting. I found myself wanting to play something like Glory to Rome instead, where the role selection is more interesting. With Imperial Settlers, it turns into "you take an action, I take an action, you take an action..." little decisions that don't really impact the other players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
The game seems to be balanced for two but plays up to four. The game takes no consideration for how the game will play with more then two players. Cards that give something when an opponent takes a certain action are extremelly strong in 4 player games and cards that allow you to attack faction buildings are cool in two player games but are bad with more.

Even when playing with two players most of the game feels like multiplayer solitaire.

The game has the illusion of being asymmetrical. The factions do have different buildings but you don't feel like you are doing anything different. Yes the Japanesse get food and Egyptians get gold but what they do with those resources doesn't feel any different. They both use them to build buildings and generate points. Even the Barbarians that focus on swords you'd think they'd be pretty aggressive and want to fight but nope. The more efficient option is to raze your own buildings to gain points which makes all the factions feel very samey.

The biggest problem is the order in which you draw cards. Through the course of a game you should be able to draw through most of your faction deck (and if you are lucky enough to get a building that allows card draw you should be able to make it easily through the whole faction deck). However, drawing certain cards at certain times has such a huge swing in how well you will do in the game. Cards tend to be either geared towards the early game or the late game and you have no control when you will get these cards. It would have been really nice if the faction deck was split into two and you'd draw from the first pile for turns 1-3 and the second pile for turns 4-5 or something like this.

I want to give the game a couple more tries with two players but will probably not play again with four.

RATING: 4
What starts out as a regular snowball engine builder with zero interesting innovations [i]stays[/i] a regular snowball engine builder with zero interesting innovations. Resources = cards + resources; repeat ad finitum; cards = VP; done. To be honest it's not all that bad, and the amount of material supplied with the game has a nice appearence. That all said and done... the game has Issues.

One is that fonts and icons are ridiculously small, and impossible to read upside down. Yes, you do need to look at what others are up to from time to time; especially if you are [i]not[/i] a player who plans on playing this title so often he knows the cards by heart. Another is that the game begins to drag seriously once rounds 4 and 5 are here. There is very little game to consider save efficiently squeezing points from the engine built up and the cards still in hand and whatever is left from the resources. (Of course, people who like engines will love this spiralling-out-of-control feeling.) And the third and most important is luck of the draw. The point to consider here is not really the average case, where it can be shown without doubt that most actions are 'balanced'. No, what needs consideration is the [i]timing[/i] of the various draws, for example when one player gets agressive cards operational earlier than the opposition, or when good card combos are able to make it earlier to the table than the opposition. The positive feedback loops in Imperial Settlers make it virtually impossible to retaliate in an appropriate fashion if not straight away. In other words: it's not a problem that nasty cards, or some good combination [i]will[/i] be drawn. No, it is that the factions will not draw these [i]at approximately the same time[/i], when doing something about it takes the least amount of effort, and when damage is still minor enough to be repaired. There is nothing in the game to deal with this discrepancy, so probably the best solution is simply to limit the number of players so that the effect, intended or not, is minimised.

All in all I find it curious to play such a game with a present element of conflict, and then read on the box that it is a cardgame with [i]civilizations to care about[/i]. Like hell we care. Shamelessly beat opposition to a bloody pulp if you have the opportunity, that's what this game is about.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Kolejna rewelacyjna gra stworzona przez Ignacego Trzewiczka, zbiera same pozytywne opinie w recenzjach we wszelkich serwisach planszówkowych. Gra jest pięknie wykonana, obrazki na kartach są opracowane z najdrobniejszymi detalami, a drewniane znaczniki nie mniej urocze. Osadnicy są grą łatwą do nauczenia, co nie znaczy nudną! 4 cywilizacje i mnóstwo kart sprawiają, że każda rozgrywka jest inna i pasjonująca. Wariant 2-osobowy jest bardzo przyjemny i nie trwa zbyt długo (ok. 40 minut). Tytuł świetnie sprawdzi się dla par, ponieważ gra zawiera i pierwiastek męski (budowa imperium, podbijanie przeciwnika), i żeński (spokojna rozbudowa, produkcja dóbr). Gra bardzo dobrze się skaluje, jedynie przy większej ilości osób wydłuża się czas rozgrywki w związku z turami innych graczy. Wszystko świetnie się dopełnia, a cywilizacje są na równym poziomie i wymagają odmiennego podejścia. Idealna również do gry solo.

RATING: 9
A fun card based game where each player is a different nation with your own deck of cards. These cards represent buildings you want to add to your civilization, combined with cards from the common deck.

Drafting each round gives you common cards, you get to draw from your faction deck each round as well and then the buildings give you the resources you need to build them, or you can sacrifice cards from hand to generate resources.

A fun, complex but easy to pick up, resource management/empire building game.

Amusing artwork makes for an accessible game, enhanced by a growing number of expansions.

RATING: 6.9
My first 2 player game took 2 hours to complete.  While there is certainly strategy you can employ, you can't really plan very far ahead because you don't know what cards you will get.  And for the most part, you have to use all your resources before the end of the round.  So it takes quite a bit of thinking to maximize use of your resources.  Also, it seems like there a few faction cards that are way over powered.  My opponent was able to play his powerful cards at the beginning of the game.  But I did not draw my powerful card until the last round.  So this caused it to be unbalanced.  If this game only took 45 minutes to play, I'd give it a thumbs up.  But considering it takes 2 hours (and I doubt it will be much quicker on a subsequent play - especially if more players are added), there are many more longer games that I prefer.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Here's why this game is a solid "fine."  First, I'm not upset about the "take that" in the form of razing, which is (i) really infrequent, (ii) often a bad idea from your own perspective, and (iii) when it is a good idea, everyone recognizes that yeah, that point producing building needs to go.  That didn't bother either of us.

What does bother me is the "I go you go" turn structure for a game with minimal interaction.  The only reason to interrupt my turns is to give you an opportunity to raze my buildings, but it's pretty rare.  Otherwise, my turns are simply putting pieces of a combo together, but bizarrely our turns have to be interwoven for some reason???

With a game like Nations it makes sense, because one action is drafting from a common display.  Imperial Settlers doesn't have that (the closest would be drawing from the shared deck, but that's all face-down).

The second flaw are the combos themselves.  Despite the variety of cards, they're really basic: make stuff, turn stuff into more stuff, turn that stuff into points.  It's pretty pedestrian, and its simplicity is hindered by the aforementioned turn structure.  I have to do all of these simple actions, but interrupted by your own simple actions.

A game like this can't compete with Race for the Galaxy and Seasons as tableau building combo games.  Cute art, yeah, but vanilla gameplay.

RATING: 8
Imperial Settlers is a tableau-building game in which you play cards that represent buildings/institutions that help you gather resources and expand your civilization. The four factors have their own unique buildings that when combined with a huge stack of common buildings, give the players a lot of choices and make their civilization grow differently each time.

The cartoon-ish characters and structures depicted on the cards are adorable, but the text is rather small. Unless you have memorized the cards, you won't know the functions of each card at a glance. That poses a problem for non-veteran players, which are probably most people. There are also many nuances to the card functions, so it leads to confusion and necessitates frequent visits to the manual and online forums. These are just minor downsides to an otherwise solid game. 

Also, the solo variant is fun and serves as a nice bonus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Each player receives a deck of own faction cards, and by drawing cards from this deck mixed with cards from the common deck they try and build an empire consisting of production cards, benefit cards and action cards. The faction cards (Roman, Egyptian, Barbarian en Japanese) are very different. Depending on which faction you play, you will play a different game.
The game lasts 5 rounds, each consisting of a card draw phase, a production phase, an action phase and a clean up phase. The action phase lasts as long as at least one player hasn't passed. Since each faction can only stock specific resources (people, gold, food, stone, wood, weapons) one has to try and spend all non-storables produced each round.
The draw of the cards is important. The order in which you receive certain cards influences your scoring ability. So you have to trust your luck. Sometimes powerful combos appear, sometimes you're just struggling to get some points on the board. This can be frustrating, but it is also what makes the game highly replayable. Even with the same faction you can end up playing a very different game depending on which cards you obtain early on in the game.
Conclusion: an enjoyable civilization builder card game.

RATING: 7
Good engine building game with a light civ theme. There are better engine builders however. Game is quite parsimonious in giving you resources, it's like running your car on fumes for miles, some people may like that. It makes the game quite unforgiving of mistakes and you will know you are losing, which again is a good and a bad thing. Game is very well supported by expansions, but there is lots of material straight out of the box. Though I do think the main thing going for this game - it is good - is perhaps the cutesy artwork which hides a relatively brutal game - hey that's my fish like - as you can burn peoples stuff down if you fancy. The resources are also quite cute - though I do wish that every resource had been wood, rather than a few things which are cardboard chits. However there are better turn x into y and get z games out there - though these are mostly worker placement game -  and hence this one doesn't get played as much as it should. Jury is still out.

Offloaded this one with no remorse.

RATING: 8
Solid civ-building card game which feels a little like a blend of Race For The Galaxy and Ora & Labora, although a little simpler than both.   The fact that resources clear each turn (apart from storage abilities) makes getting your cards played a bit of a puzzle.   The faction decks offer a lot of variability, and the game comes with a large number of cards.   Graphics and production are excellent, although the text is a bit small.   Plays very quickly after a learning game.   The ability to sack other players buildings will be loved/hated by some, but it provides most of the interaction (which is still fairly low).   Overall, perhaps not terribly original, but really well-executed and a joy to play.

(a note on the solo game:  the victory condition to qualify for points is far too easy, almost to the point of irrelevancy, so it just becomes a quest for high score.  Playable, but not really a challenge, per se.)

It's like the min-maxing at the end of a game of Agricola, except that here it's the whole game. Most resources can't be kept from round to round, which means that you have to figure out how exactly to spend everything you have in every single round, and it's extremely unpleasant. I will not be touching this one again.

RATING: 6

While I don't think removing some of the convoluted aspects of its predecessor was a bad thing, I dislike the changes they made to player interaction.  51st State, surprisingly, encourages economic player interactions, where players can use an opponent's building, trading a worker for another resource. =-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Thought 51st Stat/New Era had a ton of potential, but I just didn't like it compared to other tableau builders I've played, so I moved on. Imperial Settlers was announced. It was said to have streamlined play, text instead of iconography (I like RFTG's iconography, but 51st's was horrid), even better graphic design/artwork (imo), and unique factions! I was really excited. While it is much better than it's predecessors (again imo), I've tried it many times, but it's still not as good as some other tableau games I own. 

My biggest problem with the game is that faction deck sequence can really make-or-break your game. With faction cards, you really want your production buildings and/or card drawing in the beginning. Sadly, I've played games as the Barbarians where the 3 copies of the cards that produce 2 wood were all within the last 5 cards of the deck and that just majorly puts a hamper on things. Card draw is also really important to try and mitigate the faction deck sequence issues, but that in and of itself is a sequence issue with the common deck. If my opponent gets all of the Watchtowers and Castles (??), there is not much I can do about that. Yes, I could Raze those buildings, but that doesn't work if they just raze it from their hand. Finally, the game lasts just a little longer than I'd like it to and it usually seems like a winner can be determined by the 4th round, making the last usually inconsequential. It wouldn't matter if rounds were of equal length, but the turns naturally get longer as you build up your engine. 

This may sound really negative, but I do really like many aspects of the game. I even contemplate re-acquisition from time-to-time, but I think I've made the right decision by passing on it.

This game is currently #90, which is about a 1000 ranks too high. The game falls in the same category with Seasons: a multi-player solitaire extreme.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
The thrust of the game is hand management and efficient resource conversion that leads to a) a points engine on your end and b) the ability to throw monkey wrenches into that of your opponent(s). You do this by using cards to get resources to build cards that will give you more resources to build VP engine and attack cards. 

OK, I’m a gamer, I get it, it’s up to me to chain my cards as efficiently as possible in the evolutionary chain of “production to effects to VPs.” That can be fun. But I don’t find inspiration in this implementation. The arc of the game is set for every play; you’re going to be heads down in isolation for the first act and then look up for the last act with the Beatdown Finale. 

This last act requires you to now track your opponent's numerous powers as well as yours and is simply tedious and complicated by the unnecessarily small text. It would be marginally better if each player had the choice of building to lose himself (or ask your opponent to tell you which building they should raze and save us all time).

The whole thing feels clumsy and under-developed. The Igo-Ugo card drafting mechanism in particular feels like a gawky attempt at subtlety, as if that's supposed to add some sort of consideration to things; it doesn't. All the races do is provide the veneer of difference but it's all the same puzzle. The effect for me is a mildly interesting solitaire efficiency puzzle that I spend several turns working out and then some bitchslapping as we race to the end.

Note: I only played 2P and I can't imagine how chaotic it would be with more. I've heard complaints about card draws affecting the outcome which doesn't surprise me; just more clumsiness of the design from the Dan Brown of the hobby.
 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
When this released at GenCon at 2014 I thought "that looks neat, and I like Race for the Galaxy, but I'm not sure it's worth the money..." it is, every penny.

The asynchronous play between the factions fells well integrated, as well as the color matching/set collection aspect of the game (want apples?  Collect reds.  want workers?  Go for pinks, and so on).

I'm a big fan of tableau/engine building games, and this is no exception.  The direct attack cards bring interaction to the game that would otherwise be missing.  Also, since you collect a wood for being attacked, and most likely needed a foundation later, it doesn't always feel like a bad thing to lose your building.

If you're looking for a new card game with lots of depth, then you should look into Imperial Settlers.

RATING: 7.5
I always liked 51st State a lot, except that it takes hours to finish and is quite fiddly. But the basic mechanisms and the drive of the game appealed to me, and I generally do not like engine builders.

So playing IS was very easy for me, it's largely identical to 51st, just a touch clearer and limited to 5 rounds. A few of the anomalies in 51st are removed, and it's all smoothed out and easier to play.

Two criticisms, SUCH BLOODY SMALL WRITING ON THE CARDS!!!! Drives me crazy, the text is so bloody teeny tiny, who can reads this stuff??? And secondly, it's much more multi-player solitaire than 51st, the only inter-action is destroying an opponents building or stealing resources. At least 51st had the element of spending my people in your faction, which helps you too. So it's another engine building solitaire, but retains enough of the fluidity of 51st, it's worth playing.

RATING: 6
[b]Card-driven empire-development-lite game. Every round is one long, growing combo. Leans euro. Despite apparent asymmetry of decks, games tend to feel similar from play to play. [i]Let a friend own it.[/i][/b]

Rating history: 8|9|8|8|8|6|8...

1p - 1 play - Decent, but not too challenging.
2p - 5 plays - This is the way to play it. No downtime.
4p - 1 play - Way. Too. Much. Downtime.

10+ plays (MAR/16) - This game is pleasant to solo, and I've made peace with the fact that the decks feel similar from play to play (i.e. you see most of your deck in a game, so it's just a matter of what order you play things in).

1st play: 8. OCT/14. I enjoy Robinson Crusoe, so another game by the same designer was a must-try for me. The artwork is a style I love (chubby toddlers on the Sumo card!), slightly reminiscent of Klemens Franz mixed with NES 8-bit RPG style. 

The game is essentially a pay-to-put-in-play card game, but of course there are a few wrinkles. First, the resources you need to put cards into play are very hard to come by. Second, some cards won't be played; they can be "razed" for a quick one-time boost of resources/points. Third, some cards won't be played or razed, they'll be used to make a "deal" - which allows you an extra (precious!) resource! Fourth, while all players have access to a common pool of cards, each player has their own civilization deck that give each player a number of different ways to approach the game.

The powers the cards give you are more euro-style than american: effects tend to be minor, similar, limited to one use per round (max five per game!), and revolve around exchanges of of goods/points/gold. This is not Magic, nor even Agricola. I'm not a huge euro fan, but this simple abilities allow players to construct a lot of inter-locking half-engines to produce points, etc.

I like this game. How much I like it remains to be seen. My 4p game took 3 hours, but if I can get a 2p game under an hour, I'll like this game. I may even love it once an expansion comes out.

5th play: 8. Oct/14. This could drop to a 7 in another 10 plays. Factions are not quite as asymmetrical as I originally thought, and I can see losing enthusiasm for the game after a half dozen plays of each deck. Needs expansions with more complex abilities and a new mechanic.

[b]6 plays in[/b] and my enthusiasm is waning. Every play of the game feel too similar.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Well... I thought that nothing new could shine under the sun, boy, how mistaken I was!
Imperial Settlers is a truly truly gem. The different uses you can do of a card put its replayability to the top! You can build cards from your hand, you can make "deals" with cards from your hand, you can raze cards from your hand and from other players tableaus. The way you play this game may vary strongly depending on the people you're playing with, but, also, depending on the specific faction you and your opponents are playing with (the base game comes with four different civs/factions).

I'm not tired at all... I mean, I really like this a lot!

RATING: 8
Hármasban próbáltuk ki a játékot. Mindenkinek volt már 51st state tapasztalata így a megtanulása elég könnyen ment. Alig van különbség működésében az elődhöz képest. Láthatóan cél volt, hogy egyértelműbbek legyenek a szabályok. Ez sikerült is, bár nem volt nehéz túlszárnyalni a 51st state szabálykatyvaszát.

Az alkatrészek minősége vegyes érzelmeket keltett bennem: a nyersanyagok nagyon cuki fa alkatrészek, különösen az alma tetszett. Viszont a kártyák minősége nem túl jó. A kártyagyárban jobb kártyák készülnek. A lapok vékonykák, a nyomtatás messze nem tűéles, enyhén elmosott. Még a népek boardjának nyomtatási minősége is véleményes. A tokenek rendben vannak.

A játék most már fix 5 körből áll. Ugyanúgy lehet dealnek, expolitnak, vagy épületnek letenni egy lapot. Leginkább talán az exploit szabályai változtak: itt már rögtön megkapod a cuccot, nem kell várni a következő kör elejéig, nomeg elvileg exploitolhatod a másik játékosok felépített épületeit is. (ez lehet, hogy volt már a new eraban is, majd valaki tapasztaltabb megmondja a frankót). Azért írom, hogy elvileg, mert ez kétszer annyiba kerül, mint a saját lap exploitja, így mi az első játékban nem igazán láttuk értelmét.

Ami még érdekes volt, hogy a saját fajod lapjainak nagyon kis százalékát húzod fel a játékban, így az, hogy éppen milyen lapok kerültek a kezedbe, nagyban befolyásolja a játékodat. Ez egyfelől nagy újrajátszhatóságot jelent, másrészt, egy-egy rosszabb kéz eléggé nehézzé teheti a játékodat szerintem. Nem tudom mit lehet kezdeni azzal a szituációval pl., ha valakinek nem jut ponttermelő lap. Persze ezeket nehéz egy játék után megmondani, úgyhogy inkább csak érzések, semmint valós kritikák.

Összességében azt mondom, hogy ha már megvan és sikerült is megtanulnod a 51st state sorozat valamelyikét, akkor nincs sok értelme megvenned ezt a játékot, mivel inkább csak egy egyértelműsített változata az eredetinek. Ha viszont még nem ismered 51st state-et, de szeretnél egy elég komolyan kombózós, egyedi kártyajátékot, akkor mindenképp ezt válaszd az elődök helyett.

u.i.:
A négy faj elég más játékot igényel, így valódi variable player powers játéknak tűnik. Más a teljes paklid, más nyersanyagokat tudsz átmenekíteni a körök között, és másokat is termelsz a körök elején. A kiegyensúlyozottságukról nem mernék egy játék után komolyan nyilatkozni. Nekünk elsőre a japánok erősnek tűntek az olcsó, de ugyanúgy két pontot adó épületeikkel

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Imperial Settlers is a tableau building game that technically works but is seriously lacking some inspiration. For the most part, you are trying to build a typical resource conversion engine which is a very solitary endeavour - and then for some reason the single available opportunity for interaction is negative, allowing you to disrupt other players' plans by razing their buildings.

In general, having different factions with somewhat different starting powers, strengths and weaknesses seems like a good idea to add some variety. For the factions included in the game, however, the asymmetry seems to be so strong that you either follow the route your faction dictates or go under, ie. you're actually not in a position to choose which path you would like to take. With the strategy already chosen for you, it pretty much comes down to drawing the cards in the correct order (production buildings first, VP buildings later). In contrast to, for example, Race for the Galaxy there simply isn't enough card churn to overcome unhelpful draws.

RATING: 7
Vier Comic-Völkerfiguren mit kiddy Knollennasen treffen sich in einem fiktiv herbeigezerrten Land und balgen sich um die Vorherrschaft in Form von Siegpunkten. Dabei ist auch das Zusammentreffen historisch wackelig: Ägypter gegen Japaner?!?!! Hä?? Auch IMPERIAL SETTLERS klingt mühsam gebogen und hätte genauso gut EMPIRE FOLKS oder NATIONAL MONUMENTS heissen können. Doch Textkrampf und Völker-Mix werden von einem patienceartigen, autistischen Kartenspiel aus Polen wettgemacht: aus einem Stapel individueller Völkerkarten und einem allgemeinem Stapel legt man Ortskarten dreier Grundtypen in gewohnter Kombi-King-Manier in die eigene Auslage, um einen Motor zum Laufen zu bringen. Dabei hat jedes Volk seine Stärken und Schwächen. Das Schöne und Angenehme bei IS ist, dass es mehr taktischer als strategischer Natur ist, was dazu führt, dass sich die Züchtung von Grüblern am Tisch in Grenzen hält. Der solitäre Multiplayer zum überteuerten Verkaufspreis schreit förmlich "Erweiterung" in Form von neuen Völkern und weiteren Karten. Allerdings gibt es auch inhomogene Elemente: die Downtime kann ziemlich krass und das Zerstören von eigenen Karten durch den Gegner verheerend sein, von daher funktioniert IS am besten zu zweit. In diesem Sinne darf die Knollennase auf dem Cover darf mit ihrem Wau-Wau weiter in den Krieg ziehen.

RATING: 3
I wanted to like this game, but I couldn't get into it even after several plays with other people who enjoyed it a lot. I don't like:

0) essentially zero player interaction aside from the bizarre raze mechanic
1) how similar all the factions are to each other
2) how rushed I feel to get stuff done in only five rounds
3) the combat is ridiculously shallow and should just be taken out, or expanded
4) confusing and overly long rules text (wait, how do I raze my buildings versus other players'?)
5) lots of "I gotta remember that this building generates this resource each turn"
6) way too much text to read on the cards, which slows down the pace

The whole game just feels "off" somehow. I love 4X type games when they're computer games, so I figured I would like this game, but it's in serious need of polishing the rules and gameplay flow. Maybe a revamped 2.0 version in a couple years could convince me to try it again.

RATING: 7
THIS IS CERTAINLY NOT A CARD GAME!

Demo'ed this game this sunday at a con, I was the only one there that knew how this game worked.

The game was set up before I got there. Someone did that based on the image in the rulebook, not knowing that this is a game were you start with next to nothing, but end up with tons of cards on the table.  So my table was really too small and players had to build over and into each other.

Second, I guess because of the cute art, the organisers (who didn't know the rules nor the game) were placing "newbies" at the table.  By which I mean people who played Settlers of Catan once or twice.  For those that are calling this a "simple" game, I disagree completely.

To be clear: The fault of the above lies not with the organisation (they really did a great job), but because this game was brought in the last minute by the sponsor. Everyone (organisation and many visitors) expected it to be a not-heavy game because it says "card game" on the box, it has cute art, and you start with an empty board.


Great game though, but it's not because the rules are streamlined, this game is for beginning players.  This truly is a gamer's game.

RATING: 5
This is a bit of an unfair 5-rating in that it's a personal 5, or a 5 for me, but I see how Imperial Settlers is a good game and why others would rate it much higher. I've just come to realize that deck builders/hand builders/tableau builders where turns take longer and longer so each player can "run" their area don't appeal to me too much. This might work better as a 2-player only game.

PROS
-Theme and art are fun!

CONS
-Card abilities are not that exciting; most are just point generators.
-Gathering resources to build three or more cards in a round felt like too much of a struggle. It reminded me of City of Remnants where I just spent 2/3 of the game scraping enough together to do one small action.
-Despite the "razing" mechanic, player interaction is pretty minimal. 
-Quite hard to keep track of what others were building or working toward in a 4-player game.

RATING: 8
I like this game. Pretty simple mechanics, but many choices to be made which is neat, but can lead to some longer turns when there are too many calculations going on as far as resource usage. 

It's neat that the factions play differently from one another, though players may not know what each other have built unless they are constantly looking over at the others' play area. But players can simply announce what they are building and what the building does, so everyone can have at least some idea of potential actions/benefits the others will have at their disposal. 

Lots of cool cards to play, from both the faction and common decks. Though sometimes there can be some card luck where a player can get cards early that will have long-term benefits, while other times get cards near the end of the game that aren't useful at that point. But then you can decide to raze your crappy cards for the resources or make deals with some etc. 

I've played 4 games with others and 12 solo. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Propably my favourite game since it's the one I've played far more than any other. The rules are simple enough to explain to casual gamers and everyone I've played this with has liked it. The only other game that I've seen getting that reaction is the gateway classic Ticket To Ride. Also, the artwork is really cute. For me the visuality of a game means a lot and Imperial Settlers hits the spot perfectly.

So basically every player chooses a faction of their own and draw cards from 2 piles: common and faction decks. These cards can be used in 3 ways. They can be built as buildings, destroyed for goods or turned into "deals" ie. turned into production facilities. Players have goods, workers and raze tokens they can do the forementioned with or activate the card abilities or destroy a building from another player.

Buildings come in 3 varieties: production buildings, feature buildings (ie. "every time you do x you get x") and action buildings (ie. "use a worker to get points or goods").

That's it. All cards have their own abilities and costs and players are weighing on which cards to pick, build and destroy. It's surprisingly addictive and ego-boosting to come up with chain reactions giving you buildings, goods and points.

The game also includes a solo game where you are trying to build more faction buildings than the "virtual player" has cards in his deck. Between rounds he attacks taking the cards he hits. The solo game works very well. It's quick and fun meaning you'll be doing it a lot. I love my Mage Knight but it takes a day to complete one game. Imperial Settlers' solo game takes 20 minutes.

There's some comments complaining how "uneven" the faction cards are. I can't agree with this. I've played with all of the factions and I've never lost playing against my friends. They all have a different flow, especially the Japanese which are more of a defending faction. Whether you win or not has more to do with how you are using your cards. Having said that, luck is playing a role. There have been times when someone is getting a row of bad cards and never really getting a chance to play.

All in all a great came! Only the expansions I can not whole-heartedly recommend. Why can't we be friends and 3 is the magic number offer more cards without messing with the gameplay but the 2 new factions (Atlanteans & Aztec) I'd pass.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Don't believe the hype. The game is pretty and has nice euro mechanisms, but each race has an overpowered (and worse, easy to build) building that you may - or may not -  randomly draw during the game. If you don't draw yours, you won't win - simple. A very poor design oversight in a very promising game. Also, later turns can really balloon and drag on in endless combos of cube pushing, which adds even more frustrations for those who know they've already lost (and before you ask no, it wasn't me - I was a close second!).

RATING: 3
The most powerful buildings completly chrushes this game! They are way to cheap to build, and if you get one early in the game, for example the Roman card Engineers, their is nothing the other players can do to stop you from removing the other players more useful cards from the game (and in the case of that card, getting VPs for doing it :-/). Basically you are TOTALLY dependant on the luck of the draw in this game.

The game takes atleast one hour to long to play for what it is (both times I playes this, it took almost 3 hours!)

I would say that this game is roughly as deep as something like Takenoko, beacause a couple of lucky draws really changes the odds in your favor. In a short game like Takenoko it´s OK, but here it really is crappy.

I really like the idea of having asymmetrical factions, but it is hard to pull of designwise, and in my opinion they have not succeeded here.

And the Japanese faction seems to me to be heavily underpowered! If you play a strong building, the other players are gonna knock it down for you ASAP, beacuse Japanese faction cards are razeable. Then one might think that the Japanese faction should be compensated in some way for this, but I am yet to find the advantage they have (I have compared the different factions cards to the Japanese, and still no advantage found, quite the contrary!)

All in all I am really disappointed at this game! It feels rough and unpolished, unfinished even..

RATING: N/A
起始設定:
每人抽自己兩張
A.挑牌階段(每輪開始):
抽自己牌庫一張，公用牌開N+1張，首家先選第一輪，再來反方向尾家選。
B.生產階段:
 生產自己國家基礎資源以及Production卡所提供資源。
C.動作階段:
六個動作可以選一:
1.打出卡片，付出所需資源(依三種卡放置，公用放右邊自己國家左邊)
2.貿易，卡片反轉插入個人圖板上方底下。
3.侵略，可以侵略自己手牌(花1點武力)，得到右上角資源，卡片棄掉。(侵略對方， 花2點武力， 對方會獲得1木頭，卡片翻面變成廢墟)
4.使用Action卡動作
5.任意交換資源，以兩工人換取
6.Pass
備註:
Prodution: 生產階段，生產所需資源(所有牌打出來會做第一次生產，以及一次性Bonus獎勵)
Feature: 被動功能
Actions:需要花上面資源啟動，一回合一次。
C.清理階段:
清空所有資源，除了可以儲存的以外。

RATING: N/A
Un juego descendiente del 51st State pero reestilizado y estructurado de nuevo para hacerlo más jugable.

En síntesis es un juego de civilizaciones que se juega sólo con cartas. Contiene cuatro culturas (bárbaros, romanos, egipcios, japoneses) con sus ventajas y puntos débiles. Los jugadores deben construir estructuras que otorgan beneficios mientras intenta desarrollar un motor económico para conseguir PV´s suficientes que les permitan alcanzar la victoria.

Su grafismo me recuerda a los juegos de estrategia en tiempo real de hace unos cuantos años y en lo que destaca es en los combos de las cartas específicas de cada civilización. El conflicto directo es esencial para jugar bien aunque no es lo suficientemente duro como para dejar fuera del juego a nadie.

Lo que más me gusta es la idea de usar una carta de muchas maneras. Puedes construirla, firmar un pacto o saquearla y todo ello te permitirá obtener beneficios
