=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.74
[b]2 Player Review:[/b] 

Imperial Settlers is 51st State refined. I don't know how else to describe it unless it's to say it's been refined a little too much so. It's ironed out all the bugs and iconography of the original masterpiece, but has lost a lot of the original games complexity in the process. It's tricky to critique the game as overall I still prefer the original, but the combination of artwork, speed of play and the ability to quickly teach it to anyone has definitely pushed it ahead. 
However, it has quickly become a solo regular and I might burn out on the inherent simple-minded strategies of each race quickly. So unless a greater variety of strategies show themselves with add-on packs, or if they don't release at least one new race a year, it could quickly drop below 51st, especially since they have just announced the revamped Master Set.

[b]Pros:[/b]

- Very cute artwork and fantastic resource components
- Ease and speed of play 
- The ability to teach a civ-building game to anyone

[b]Cons:[/b]

- Strategies become very repetitive after 20ish plays, even with the "Why Can't We Be Friends?" expansion
- Desperately needs more variety. 
 
[b]2015 Rating:[/b] 9.45 [b]Rank:[/b] 10 [b]Plays:[/b] 21

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Similar level (and mechanics) as in 51st State/New Era/Winter series, but the feeling is different.

IS is simplified.  It offers less choices and rules subtleties plus somewhat easier decisions.  The variability is smaller.  And when it presents itself it's not always from the bright side (like some folks gettig their most powerful faction buildings right from the beginning while others - late or not at all).  Randomness is introduced/increased by – paradoxally - fixed faction decks.  They were supposed to provide some stability/predictability, but at the same time they fuel the randomness: the order of drawing particular cards is very important for the final score...  Still, an experienced player can overcome it (to a substantial degree) with a good play so it's not so big deal. 

The game is a sheer optimization fun and that's why I recommend it.

RATING: 8.9
Uit verkoop Bokkensprong 19/01/2018. EUR 17, waarvan de helft met bokskes betaald.
Elk speelt met een volk (Romeinen, Barbaren, Egyptenaren, Japanners) 5 ronden. Per ronde krijg je kaarten (eigen volk-kaarten en algemene kaarten) en goederen (arbeiders, voedsel, hout, steen, zwaard, schild) en speel je acties tot iedereen past. Je verdient punten met gebouwde kaarten (2 ptn voor gebouwde volk-kaarten, 1 pt voor gebouwde algemene kaarten) en voor sommige acties (vb. een punt per keer dat je plundert). Wie het meeste punten haalt, wint. 
Tof spel voor ietwat tactische spelers.

RATING: 6
Suffers from a bit of randomness in the cards. Twice I've played against a barbarians player who has gotten four instances of the "1 point per raze" card who then proceeded to just collect swords and common cards and absolutely demolished the rest of us in points. Four points for a card and a sword as many times in a turn as you can afford is absurd. But when you get your deck doing what it's supposed to be doing it's a ton of fun. But even if you do get all the right cards fast enough, there are enough ways for opponents to blow up your stuff that it never feels safe. Sort of a weird hybrid of a resource management euro with a few brutal take that cards thrown in.

Update: I've reduced the rating by half a point since every time someone has suggested this over the last few months I've vetoed it. I think I just always end up getting frustrated by the card draw.

RATING: 9.4
2.7
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
One of my least favourite things in games is when I need to know what abilities that other players have that can affect me or that will affect my strategy, but that information is not possible to see at a glance without very good game knowledge or by getting up, walking around the table, and physically reading everything that is in front of the other players.

In this game each faction has a few cards that can mess with other players, you need to know immediately when these cards are on the table. You also need to know what resource/points engine cards that the other players have in play for when you need to interrupt those engines. Finally(?) you also need to know what razable cards the other players have, their raze bonus and how powerful the card is.

The game plays great and has lots of nice mechanics as a card driven civ builder, but it takes a long time to play with 4 players (well over 3hrs), it's not so bad in that each players turn is fairly quick, so the downtime between turns isn't a problem, but the number of turns players can take in the latter rounds is where the ridiculous game length comes in.

Most of the luck of the card draw can be mitigated by having a lot of card draw and/or using lots of workers to draw cards, but unfortunately without the ability to search your deck for the important early game engine cards, you can be at a big dissadvantage if they are all at the bottom of the deck.

As for how cuthroat the game is, other than razing ability, each faction has:
Barbarians: steal resources, destroy deals
Egyptians: block locations, temporarily steal locations (can do a combination of these two 6 times per round for an additional 1gold each time if 2 swimming pool's are out)
Romans: steal deals, destroy locations
Japanese: destroy deals, destroy locations

Most of these attacking actions are cost effective so there is no reason not to do it every round, the problem is that whoever gets the destroy location ability card out first will have the upper hand (except the egyptians who steal/block and therefore will rely on turn order), especially if it takes several rounds before the other players draw their attacking cards.

This game takes too long as it is, so I won't be playing 4player again, even 3 is at the limit, in reality it's probably one of the best solo games around. But in multiplayer, I can't see this game being a long term keeper given the vicousness of the attacking cards (they feel imbalanced, overpowered, and like they are artificially adding interaction to a game that does need the interaction, but they don't do a very good job), but I shall have to play this a few more times with everyone knowing what they're doing before deciding for sure.

RATING: 5.5
2014-10-31

Initial Rating: 7.0 (October 2014)

At last I got to play Imperial Settlers. I didn’t have high expectations because I read the game was similar to 51st State, an ok game for me. To be perfectly honest I didn’t find the games much alike BUT I played 51st State a long time ago, now I want to try it again.

The art, components and overall presentation of the game are gorgeous, (Spoiler Alert) love the hidden ninjas in the box :D. Easy to teach. The playtime is relatively short and the game flows very quickly with little downtime. I think it is best with 2 players, but I would play with any number of players although with more than 2 players there can be “an attack the leader” effect that I have come to dislike. Also with 4 players I feel a bit of downtime.

The theme does nothing for me.

Imperial Settlers is a very good combo card game, it has some luck of the draw but I think it can be mitigated in many ways (production, deals, attacks) so for now I´m very happy with it. I love card games that have interesting decisions, and this one does because most cards can have multiple purposes, you can build, raze and make deals. Without spending cards you also have interesting decisions, you can attack or you can use your population as a joker. Love the drafting phases.

At first blush, it seems some of the decks are stronger than others (Egyptians and Barbarians), but I really don’t have enough plays to see if this is true. 

As it is a card game, it is easily expandable and I would love to have more factions in the game.

Bottom line, a good game I want to explore further. It´s like a simpler version of RFTG. I will buy it in the near future.

RATING: 5
This would work better as a two player game, and I would likely rate it quite a bit higher in that context, perhaps a 7.

I am not a fan of the balance through player conflict design. It leads to the dreaded meta-game discussion of who is winning and the unavoidable king-making. 

The factions do not seem to have much depth. They each seem to rely on a few key cards and combos, making the early card draws hugely important. Drawing into your factions main point engine early is a huge advantage. The solution is to beat this player down with negative player interaction, which I don't enjoy.

RATING: 8
---------------------------------------
[b]My thoughts on Imperial Settlers[/b]
Imperial Settlers is a medium weight civ building game with an emphasis on card tableau building and resource management. You can either play the Romans, the Egyptians, the barbarians, or the Japanese. Each faction has a unique play style and its own deck of cards. There is also a common card deck that is accessible for every player. The cards can be used for multiple purposes: to build as buildings, to trash (raze) for 1-2 resources, or to use as a deal (thus providing you with a certain resource at the start of each turn). The game lasts for 5 rounds
I pre-ordered Imperial Settlers and picked up a copy at Essen 2014. Although the theme isn't very original, I really like the look and feel of this game. The artwork is incredibly cool and reminds me of some real time strategy PC games. There is a nice level of direct player interaction, as you can raze each others' buildings. The decisions regarding your cardplay can be very interesting. You are trying to figure out certain card combinations that will provide you with an resource engine that should eventually start producing a lot of victory points. Once you have a viable engine going, the game can get a bit repetitive, though. Also, I don't like that turns get longer and longer with each round, and its seems that some games of Imperial Settlers might overstay its welcome because of that. Other than that, a solid game.

[b]Star ranking[/b]
Fun: :star::star::star::star::nostar:
Satisfaction: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar: 
Esthetics: :star::star::star::star::star:
Replayability: :star::star::star::halfstar::nostar: It might need expansions that provide more cards for the existing factions and also new factions to explore.
[b]Verdict: Not essential, traded away.[/b]
---------------------------------------
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
On our initial play, we made the mistake of playing this like other euro-ish civ builders; keeping to ourselves and only occasionally poking the other players. This is not how the game is meant to be played.

What a difference some conflict makes. When played as intended, interfering with other cultures to slow their progress, it is a phenomenally engaging game. Player turns are quick as you are only allowed one action, and as a result the game clips along at a great pace. Depending on what your opponent(s) have built, the security of your buildings may be fleeting - so you need to be careful about how you plan your turns. It's very easy to have a big scoring turn shut down by the machinations of clever opponents.

Each culture plays a little bit differently, and lends a fair amount of replayability; certain to be enhanced by expansions. A runaway leader issue is certainly *possible*, but if everyone is keeping each other in check properly, it's unlikely to happen. 

Addressing the 'luck of the cards' complaint; looking through the culture deck after a game, a common phrase around our table has been 'if only I'd had that at the beginning of the game!' It's *possible* for someone to draw their deck in an 'ideal order' so as to have a perfect start, that develops into an unbeatable lead. But the chances of something like that happening are so slim as to be nonexistent. Furthermore, it ignores the influence of other players on their development.

Long story short, if you're not playing this while f**king with other players... You're playing it wrong. Conflict is one of the main ingredients that helps make this game great.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After three games I must correct my initial impression regarding razing. When playing the Barbarians, it's an essential part of the strategy. Especially the Japanese can suffer big time from this. Having played all factions except for the Romans, I had the most fun with the Barbarians. The Japanese can be fine when there's no too aggressive player around. The Egyptians seem a bit boring. Getting more cards early is key for any faction.
---
Mostly a multiplayer solitaire tableau building game. The only interactive action of razing opponents’ buildings was rarely used in our game. As the cards cycle slowly through the hand, it’s more a tactical puzzle to make the best out of your hand as opposed to a strategic tableau builder like RFTG. 
I like it but I’m a bit afraid that the order of the cards in each civilization’s own stack is too decisive if players are reasonably competent in solving their puzzle. In a game, probably less than half of a stack will be played through and when a few key cards needed to create synergies don’t show up there’s nothing to be done.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
In our plays, the Romans have steamrolled the competition. I really have an issue with the most valuable (faction) cards being invincible. That makes it very luck based as the right faction cards at the right time can win the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is probably one of my favorite games that I don't get to win in much if any.  The game is chuck full of choices on each of your turns, and every choice just feels good.  You can use a card in a couple of ways.  So a hand of just four cards can net you roughly eight options.  You can build, make a deal, or raze common cards.  This is on top of the things you can do with your resources like meeples.

The game can play fast with the right group, but those that like to AP will grind this game to a halt.  That's the one gripe I have with it, but it's not the game's fault.

This game is very similar to 51st State, but the choice between the two for most is the asymmetry to the factions.  I'm for the asymmetry purely for the fact that you feel unique when playing the game.

The capability for direct player interaction adds to this game, and takes it a step up from an engine builder.  The game also balances it out by giving the attacked player resources when picked on as well.  You gain a foundation for other buildings, and a wood for losing your building.  This is great for even peaceful players to get acquainted in this game.

The components are great, the illustrations are in a a very cartoony style, and everything fits well.  There are those that had said the text is small, and that might bother some, but I have no issues with the text, and the symbolism is great so it makes it easy to recognize what does what.

Bottom line, this game has high replay due to the fact that you probably won't go through your entire deck, and commons are so varied game to game you won't be locked in to a certain strategy in back to back games.  Each time you draft cards, one is discarded, so you'll almost always have 1/4 (3 players) of your cards going to discard without ever seeing play just form that alone.

With high replayability, high production value, good expansion support, fast setup, low down time, there is little I can say about this game to deter someone who is curious.  The only thing I would say is, if you hate asymmetrical games, you might want to go with 51st State.  If you ARE about asymmetrical games though, it does not get much better than this.

RATING: 8
I played this twice with my wife, Barbarians vs. Romans. Here is the primary issue I have: You start with two faction cards and draw one per round. This means that you may only get 7 of your 30 faction specific cards per game. Even if you trade resources to draw cards (which would mean that you would not have enough resources to build them) or happen to draw a common card that produces cards as a resource, you will likely only draw 12 of your faction specific cards. That means that more than half of you deck goes unused. I find this somewhat frustrating.

I am looking forward to playing this more and seeing if this is a deliberate design decision so that each of your plays requires you to adapt your strategy. I will update.

4/8/16 Played this several times solo and I really enjoy it. Looking forward to a campaign. 

Update 5/25/2016: I primarily play this solo and love it. Bumped from a 6 to a 7. 

4/1/2017: Adding the Atlanteans and Aztecs really ups this game for me. Loving it more and more. Moved from 7 to 8.

RATING: 7
Imperial Settlers is a card game that lets players lead one of the four factions and build empires by placing buildings, then sending workers to those buildings to acquire new resources and abilities. The game is played over five rounds during which players take various actions in order to explore new lands, build buildings, trade resources, conquer enemies, and thus score victory points.

FIRST IMPRESSIONS:

There is also resource generation used to construct these buildings.

RATING: 4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The first turn of my first game we had no idea what cards to draft, but that's ok since we quickly got the hang of it and I like how it is still much simpler in the early rounds since you have less buildings in play, which helps you ease you into the game.

There is some interaction outside the card drafting and razing, however by mid game it was hard to keep track of what everyone else could do at least in a 3 player game as there were too many cards on the table, making it difficult to know what everyone is capable of and which cards are key to their engine. However it is still possible to play this game almost as multiplayer solitaire in some situations.

The iconography is pretty intuitive, and the basic rules are pretty simple, it's mainly dealing with what card should I play and it what order that the decisions become difficult.

The factions feel pretty different, gaining different resources more readily and having some unique powers within their cards. However, there are still too many cards that feel like I'm just shuffling resources around to eventually be converted to points.

I feel like setting up a good draw engine is important, as relying on the luck of the draw to get the best cards for your engine is inefficient since you can only draw one card from your faction deck per turn, and there is no drafting mechanic for it. In fact, this game has a very strong engine building feel in general despite only lasting 5 rounds.

I've seen a lot of people saying they weren't a fan of razing, but honestly since you need to destroy your own buildings to play most of your faction buildings anyways getting razed isn't that bad, and in a game with more than 2 players can actually benefit you since you gain a wood for getting razed, while not all other players benefit.

3 players seems to work pretty well. I've only played 1 2 player game so far, but it didn't seem to have that much of a better experience.

The Japanese appear to be the hardest faction to play since their faction buildings can be razed, and they need to use samurai to protect them. As recommended in the rule book the Romans and especially the Barbarians (due to their ability to get a ton of resources) appear to be the easiest to play

I feel like this game sometimes has a runaway leader problem unless it is a 3+ player game with the runaway leader playing Japan. A person who designs a good engine can draw a lot of cards, and thus has a higher chance to get their victory point generating cards. Therefore, getting good cards to be able to produce the resources you need in the beginning is crucial. While the card drafting of the shared deck helps alleviate this a bit, there is still some reliance of the luck of the draw early game.

I am not a fan of the solo variant, as at least with my play style the dummy player doesn't seem to change up the game that much, making it feel like I am just off on my own building my own VP engine, abstracting the game to pushing wooden pieces around, instead of a tense me versus the game. Maybe if I play again with the Japanese this will change, but then the whole Samurai mechanic of the Japanese is mostly nullified.

EDIT: Depending on play style this game can devolve to multi-player solitaire. 2 players is definitely shorter than with 3, but besides preventing unwanted ganging up I don't really like it anymore with 3 (despite the BGG consensus). 
Even with the Japanese and the campaign mode I don't really like the solo variant. It definitely scratches an engine building itch, but it doesn't feel like I am playing against anything, more like I am just doing my own little thing, with some random event off to the side that sometimes messes with my engine (and yes I know you can mitigate by not leaving many common buildings at the end of each round). Some of the campaign provinces add significant variation to the game, but as your empire burgeons their effects make the game fiddly.

EDIT 2: So after I got rid of the game, I realized I hadn't  noticed that you get resources immediately when you build production buildings and make deals too, which is a pretty similar rule to miss, although having now played it with the correct rules, I don't know if I like it more, and the general feel of the game is the same either way. While probably more balanced (not as bad if you get production buildings later, making deals which previously seemed very last resort and some buildings that seem almost never worth it seem stronger now), you get an engine building feel sooner, and less harsh on new players, it makes the game quite a bit longer and doesn't reward long term planning as much. I think ideally I would like the game to be rebalanced around my previous incorrect rules. At least the real rules was satisfying in the fact that you can draw my entire faction deck, and it explains why I was getting such low solo scores?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Maybe I went into it with too big expectations, but it was a let down. The art is fantastic and fits the theme very well, but the gameplay didn't do it for me. First of all, it's a card game, so it's quite random. I enjoy quick games with lots of randomness, but not long ones. I would argue this was a LOT more random than Race of the Galaxy (which has many ways to mitigate randomness through exploration) and it played in 4-5x the time. Drawing cards with great synergy at the wrong time, or victory point cards in the early turns was very annoying.

The gameplay itself started from very simple (almost too simple) in the first turn, to insanely complicated in the last turn with all sorts of cards triggering in a tableau that hardly fits the table.

Worst of all, this was a heads-down experience. I never cared what my opponents were doing, except glancing at their buildings to see what I could/should raze. And that was the main player interaction: Razing each other buildings. I get why it's there, but it's not any fun (either doing it or receiving it). I'd rather have a game that is self-balancing rather than force players to bash the leader.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I love games that are fun and leave me with a sense of accomplishment even when I lose.  Imperial Settlers does just that.  Even when I lose, it is satisfying to view the empire I have created sprawled across the table.  

I also love the asymmetrical nature of the factions.  The rulebook states that there will eventually be a way to customize your faction deck.  Add to this the promise of new civilizations in the future, I can foresee that this gem is going to hit the table frequently and will likely remain one of my favorite games.  

I would highly recommend the drafting variant during the lookout phase (acquire cards phase).  Not only is it more entertaining, it also makes the phase fair for each player.

Some have dissed Imperial Settlers, claiming that it has a runaway leader problem.  Keep in mind that it is a card game, so luck of the draw can be a factor.  The runaway leader can also emerge easier if players are new to the game.  However, the more players know the game and the more they focus on drawing and building as many cards as possible from their faction deck, the less likely a runaway leader will emerge because each deck has the cards necessary to combo into huge points.

My only complaint against Imperial Settlers is that every game has ended with me thinking, “If we could just go one more round I could pull off something truly epic.”  But, this is minor and I suspect it may be an issue caused more by inefficiency of gameplay, and not knowing the faction decks well, than it is an inherent flaw of the game.  

I cannot recommend this game enough.  It is an instant classic and in my opinion should be a Spiel des Jahres nominee.  You owe it to yourself to give it a try!

RATING: 7
Very likable game after just one play (as the Japanese).
The artwork is cute and the ressource tokens are very nicely done. They rolled a fumble in making those tiny flat scoring markers, but they are easily substituted.
The print on the cards are, however, ridiculously small, which makes it impossible to find out what the other players have without asking all the time. And you need to ask, because the game is player balanced, and you need to trash the leaders best stuff or else he'll run off exponentially. The microtype and its consequences is why I don't start it off at 8.

Next game (as the Romans): This game sucks!

Third game (as the egyptians): Combolicious and I almost won with 87 points.

Conclusion:
The Romans have lost big time in all three games we have played, so until a 5th better balanced faction appears, they are out. 
The game is player balanced, but not enough to compensate for a shitty starting hand - or a very good one. There's a huge difference in getting your Pyramids and Caravans early or late - a difference that in my opinion /will/ make you win or loose if you otherwise play decently.
Also, due to the player balancing, it is very important to keep watch on your opponents playfields, but that's almost impossible, unless you have superhuman eyesight or perfect retention.

However, the game remains a solid 7, because in spite of the drawbacks it can be a lot of fun to make all the cogs & wheels fall into place (as long as you're not playing the Romans, in which case they will fall to the floor instead).

RATING: 10
+Tableau building

+Engine building w/ a great ramp up effect.  You spend the first few turns building an engine and then start executing it the last round or two. Feels good when all the pieces are in place.
+Combos
+Simple rules, but thinky game play
+Multi use cards

This is an asymmetrical game so you need to play to your faction's strength, if you want to win.  This makes the game highly re-playable, but also introduces a bit of a learning curve.  Add in deck building and you'll be able to enjoy this for many, many plays.

RATING: 7
Really enjoyed this game when I first played it multiplayer.  A very nice combination of tableau building, card drafting, hand and resource management.  Plus plenty of player interaction and great artwork, with different races having their own themes. 

Traded in a copy thinking that I might get some good solo play out of it - highly regarded.  4 solo plays in and I decided to trade it on.  5 more solo plays just to try out the different factions.

The solo game was very samey, and very random.  Basically if you don't draw a sword producing card early you are likely to lose.  It is possible to build buildings which are less likely to be attacked, but this again requires luck of the draw.  After these solo plays, the theme came across as a bit thin.  Looking at the solo campaign variant, the designer's justificaitn for releasing the campaign mode struck me:  "Everybody calls Imperial Settlers light civ game. It is not a civ game. It has no technology tree. It has no wonders. It has no leaders. Yes, some of this will come later in the expansions, but still – I don’t think about this game as a civ game. It is an engine building card game. Without technology and time progress I don’t think it might be called civ."

I completely agree.  What results is an engine building card game that happens to have cutesy Settlers (computer game) artwork.  The output from the buildings logically make sense, but the theme ends up feeling a bit hollow.  The game feels less like building an empire and more just converting stuff into other stuff into points with a lack of motivation behind doing so.  It just can't compare with the theme / tension / atmosphere of some of the other solo games I've got such as LotR, or the better mechanics and theme of Race for the Galaxy (the tableau builder I'll be holding on to).   Can't see it getting any play so time to trade it on.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
1-4 Players
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Awesome! I played not knowing fully what to expect. Even though the game comes in a big box, with lots of wooden components and plenty of cardboard tokens, it really is a card game. And it's also a resource managing game. And an engine building game. And a tableau building game. It comes with four factions, each with a different faction deck, which contains cards that are used to make big scoring combos. Even though it allows you to play lightly, every faction has their own best strategy and optimized plays that will allow players to score the most points. It is indeed a very strategic game. And it can run quite long as well. This may not be for you if: you don't enjoy heavy resource management; you prefer more thematic games; you don't like long games; you really need to roll dice; you don't like games that heavily favor more experient and skilled players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Players have their own unique deck of cards, corresponding to the settlers they take: Rome, Japan, Egypt or Barbarians. In this card driven game, players play down cards to get more resources and earn VPs. The interaction comes through swords, and you can raze an opponent's card. There is also a mini draft of cards from a common deck of cards. 

It's an interesting game. The first game, you'll feel like it is solitaire, since the card text is hard to read over the table, so you just have to be aware of what your opponent's cards do. With experience, it should get better. I'd like to try all the decks to see what they do. 

Some cards have varying abilities and sometimes it is not clear how to interpret the card. There might be some downtime with more players.  This game has the potential to be good, and some people really like it, but I'm just in the category of willing to play again.

RATING: 10
This game just feel so good to play.On the first round, you might take three turns: by round five, you might be taking 10 or more!

Even first-time players will end up building their civilization into a little machine that allows your turns to be more and more productive. Once you understand the way production buildings work, you can't help but strategizing as you figure out which order to play your cards in. 

I cannot wait for the expansions, but this also has a tremendous amount of variety right out of the box with four factions to master and way more cards than you will see in most games (if you push it, you can draw almost your entire faction deck, but if everyone is doing this, you will still probably not see all of the common deck). Of course, the game is inherently hyper-variable since playing well depends on timing and your cards will come up in a different order each time you play. 

The most satisfying engine-building game ever, and it plays two in about an hour. An elegant masterpiece!

RATING: 9
This is one of my favorite games.

It's an empire builder that uses decks unique to each player faction as well as a deck of common cards from which all players will draft.  There is not a whole lot of player interaction, but there is just enough to make it feel like we are not all playing solitaire without allowing people to regularly or devastatingly mess with my little engine.

The cards do a good job of sticking with their theme, both in art and text.  This means that each card looks like and does what the title of the card would indicate.

My only real problem with it is that the card text is tiny, so it is often difficult to keep track of what everyone can do, and can even be easy for some people to forget what is in their own empire!

That said, I have played it more than almost any other game of its length (about 20 mins per player for people who have played before), and still am not tired of it.

RATING: 6
For a civilization building game, Imperial Settlers' Building requirements are easy to understand, its endgame scoring is simple to tally up, and warfare between players is not abstract.

I especially like the player versus player combat as it is handled very equitably. The attacker may reap a benefit, but the victim isn't left entirely empty-handed.

I have complaints about the selection of civilizations to choose from. Romans and Egyptians I get; they are historical civilizations from a similar era. The Japanese are a stretch though as a definable civilization they are about a thousand years separated from the Romans and Egyptians. The Barbarians as they exist in the game are entirely fictitious. With a slight alteration they could have been the Norsemen/Vikings or Mongolians and that would have been more thematic. Plus, when having a variety of civilizations to choose from adds so much to its appeal and staying power, it is pretty sad that so few were available in this initial offering. Portal should have taken a note from Terra Mystica, Summoner Wars: Master Set, Small World, etc. and not released IS without having included more races, like twice as many.

Unfortunately, IS needed more play-testing. The factions are unbalanced, scores can swing wildly due to bad card draws, and there are some really mean-spirited cards that can suck the fun out of the game when used round-after-round.

RATING: 7.2
In its core it's an asymetrical game about making combos with cards. Every player has a different civilization and they all draw cards from a common deck and their own private deck, with special cards, more powerful, depicting the characteristics of his civilization.

The graphic design is just OK, but quite ineffective: the icons and texts are too small, and the illustrations a too similar. They are impossible to distinguish from afar, it makes it more difficult to follow what your rivals are doing.

You take most of the decisions at the start of your turn, depending on what cards and resources you have. It's quite straightforward, because each card only has 1-2 uyses at most, it's basically a question of deciding the best build order and a few things more. 

But it's very entertaining. You start with nothing, just a basic production, and each turn your civilization grows exponentially. At the end you'll have plenty of buildings around.

I think the replayability is quite low. I almost depleted my roman deck in my first play and I did not see enough cards to change the global strategy consistenly. It's a very expandable game.

It's definitly a game for 2 players, I would say 3 at most. Otherwise, it's very difficult to follow what your rivals are doing.

First impressions [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/173073/item/3515837#item3515837]here[/url]

RATING: 8.5
規則:
*隨機起始，起始手牌兩張共用牌兩張派系，一個防禦標記
*金幣是可抵木頭石頭食物，擁有資源是公開資訊，牌庫抽完把棄牌推洗進去，派系牌庫抽完不補(新規則)
1.瞭望階段獲得三張牌，抽派系一張，共用人數加一張順時針選，最後一張棄掉，在一次改逆時鐘選，手牌無上限
2.生產階段，圖版、派系交易(上方)、生產地點，生產物品
*抽牌時都可選擇共用或派系牌
3.行動階段 如果有玩家pass之後不會在被影響，包括攻擊，防御指示物要輪到該玩家才可使用不算行動，保護建築需要多一把劍才能拆
*建造地點 付資源建造，生產建築會馬上得到資源，需要房子的可移掉任何一個蓋好的房子
*進行交易 棄掉一個食物把派系卡插到上面，馬上得到資源
*拆除 拆手上的付一劍，別人的付兩劍，拿拆除得到的資源，共用地點被拆翻轉變成基地(建築派系地點可用)且被拆玩家得到一木
*使用地點 付資源到地點上並使用，清除階段清掉，沒特別說每輪只能用一次，可啟動兩次的可一次付資源用完兩次
*兩個工人換資源(木石食)或抽牌 可一次用多個工人換多個資源(任意組合)
4.清除階段 該回合沒儲存地點的資源棄掉，最後一輪跳過，物品打破平手，起始玩家換人
最終記分 共用地點一分 派系地點兩分，平手比物品工人數 在來比卡牌數
*日本人可以用工人為武士防禦派系建築，一張派系牌一個工人，清除階段不會清掉
*抽牌變體
1.共用牌改成輪抽(抽3取一，傳2取一結束)
2.2人時開五張，兩人就按順序抽一張，最後一張棄掉
  3~4人則起始抽一張後，每個剩下的從四張選一張(每次都補到四張)，在由起始從三張選一張，最後一位二選一
*和平變體 抽牌時先多抽一張共用牌，不能打人除非是派系建築
*單人 洗混16張每回合開一張攻擊卡決定哪個派系地點會被拆
抽共用牌時四張選一 三張暗抽一給對手，在二選一 最後再給對手，拆對手建築時不用翻城基地，直接棄掉 對手攻擊時照攻擊卡上方的資源選擇共用建築拆，由最新的攻擊卡開始縮限範圍，拆除順位行動特殊生產，在來是最貴的，先比資源總數，在來石頭比木頭貴，用來拆除的兩張攻擊卡放到攻擊卡堆底部，被拆的建築變成對手的建築，沒有基地跟補償，五輪後，如果全部建築比對手多就獲勝，可以記分

RATING: 6
I was reticent to try this card-based civilization game, mostly because it just didn't seem like my type of game. First off, let me say that the game is brilliantly-designed; from the custom-carved wooden bits, to the synergy between cards and factions, Imperial Settlers is a great game for players looking for re-playability and card combos. But I can't see myself playing this game with more than two people and not tearing my hair out. While your turns are limited to just one action, the endless possibilities of how and where a card is played allows for too much AP to keep from people from enjoying the game. Maybe I'm a bit spoiled with a lot of the card-based games being streamlined and simplified by a sexy new app (Hearthstone, Magic), it would simplify a lot of your decisions and make everything more apparent for your strategy. Imperial Settlers is the AP juggernaut that everyone is afraid of: the game is four-person solitaire with no deference or care given to anyone around you. While the artwork and components are delightful and cute, the flow of the game is too much for me to recommend to any group of players above two.

RATING: 8
There's some interesting takes here on the engine building genre, mainly around the choices made on which cards to burn for their resources, which to build for their ongoing benefits, and which of your high-value cards you need to sacrifice to transform into resource producers. There's luck involved in what cards you draw, and therefore what combos to build (and protect), but with many choices on how to process those cards, careful play will do well. With so many choices comes the inevitable analysis downtime, and that can be significant - the down-point of the game. You need to expect that your best "common" buildings are going to get razed by an opponent, so there's a bit of take-that, but at least the person hit gets some compensation and the hitting mostly goes around the table. But it does present a hit-the-leader issue which can problematically decide games. Engine building is a well-worn path in game design and the card effects mostly follow the formula - get resources, turn those resources into either VPs or more resources. The game in feel reminds me of Homesteaders, where you have a ton of ways to convert your resources into different resources to get the buildings which will make your VP conversion as efficient as possible. Both games can get a bit head-spinny, which is not a bad thing if everyone's prepared for it. Having said all that, the game presents cleanly and provides a lot of avenues to explore, which therefore provides plenty of upside for repeat play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Another 10 for another game of Portal awesomeness !

Excellent 2 player game although it plays upto four...it also has a strong solitaire game ...I will only ever play either solitaire or 2p so can't comment on how it plays with more than 2 players.

One of the most exciting aspects is the promised periodic releases of expansions for the game through the introduction of more cards along with new civilisations this will only be a good thing ...adding more variety, and a bit of deck building/ tweaking marvellous !

Art and components ...the art will annoy some or them off the game because of its slightly comic cartoonish nature ...I happen to love this type of art...so it's a solid plus for me ...as to the components ...well here I have two downers, and they are both related to the cards...and when I say downers that's probally over stating it, as I have rated this game a deserving ten...the font size on the cards could do with coming up a couple of notches which I am sure can be done with future card releases if publisher desires it ...and the card stock ...this has always in my opinion been a slight failing in Portal games ...please please move onto linen finished cards it feels and works much better... I know it might be a tad more expensive but I would rather pay a bit more for the game and have lovely linen finished cards rather than know automatically if it's a Portal game I will need to buy sleeves for the cards automatcally ...I'd rather not have to sleeve my cards ...I guess it's too late now for this game but how about next years Essen release ...always assuming there is one ?

Game markers, player boards ...excellent cardboard nice an sturdy perfect thickness ...nice vibrant colours used...nice wooden pieces as well...

Rule book ...well written, well laid out and very colourful ...but too big would be much nicer A4 format ...much easier to handle...

So, over all 8/10 for components ( dropped two because of cards)

RATING: 7
Imperial Settlers is a pretty simple resource management card game, where you play cards to gain resources and convert them into other resources.  It's definitely more streamlined than its predecessor 51st State, in that cards are generally usable once per turn (instead of X times per game) and have clean iconology (albeit with tiny text).  That said, there wasn't anything about the game that really felt special, and the ability to destroy other people's cards left a sour taste in my mouth.  I haven't played enough to judge, but I'm also not convinced that the factions are balanced.

(2 plays)

RATING: 4
Update: Alright, well after initially really liking it but still being somewhat cautious about it, it's dropped in rating from an 8 to a 6. The game is far too dependent on getting the right cards. I was absolutely hosed as the Romans and got 1 production building for 2/3rds of the game. I was able to pick up steam by the end of the 4th turn and 5th, but I was almost 40 points behind at the end. This game improved the overall clarity of 51st State, but it lost it's soul.



Not as good as 51st State/New Era, but it is smoother and easier to get to the table. I liked the different factions and the artwork is beautiful. No where nearly as aggressive as people are making it out to be. Sure you attack someones location, but you have to typically turn in a common location anyways to build a faction location. Except someone chose it for you.

My only complaint about the game is the extremely tiny font used and the very small icons used. I feel like they wasted a ton of space on the card and could have doubled the font size to make it at least legible from across the table. I also feel like this is another game that could have benefited greatly from iconography. 51st State is all icons and it becomes very easy to read across the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
LOVE this game. Simple, Elegant and so much to think about when playing. The streamlined iconagraphy and card descriptions make it so easy to learn. You spend time playing and not trying to figure out rules. Player interaction is pretty good to as you are thinking about what to defend and what to "raze". Strategies are so diverse and dynamic in this one.

GF says that the "euro/resource engine/buildup type" of games are not her favorite (she despises agricola) but she enjoys this one.

3rd game: she likes it even more. We were definitely more aggressive with each other and it didn't feel unfair. If she razed my locations I felt like: "ok, no problem I'll tweak my strategy a little"... The get one wood back is such a nice touch and mitigates the loss. It's a good balance of psychological rewards and soft losses... This game doesn't have many things that ruin the fun and even the aggressive stuff is reasonable.

Overall:

This game has serious fun factor. So much to do all the time... 5 rounds of fun-fiddly goodness is very satisfying.

1: Barbarrians vs Romans : barbarians
2: Romans vs Barbarrians : barbarians
3: Romans vs Japanese : Romans

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a really nice resource management game, where you are drawing cards from your personal asymmetrical faction deck and drafting cards from a general supply deck, to formulate a strategy, farm points and build your engine. There's a lot of attacking other players, but you can only take out their general deck stuff and not their powerful faction buildings. The faction buildings in most cases require you to take our your own stuff, so it's sort of acknowledged that you having that logging mill is transitory. I had a great time playing this. Punchy euros are something of a favorite of mine.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:nostar: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment

RATING: N/A
UPDATE: I rated this a 4 but we were playing incorrectly! If you use a card's power to draw a card, you can draw from either your personal deck OR the common deck! In this game, the cards from your personal deck are far more interesting!

However, the game is still a cube-exchange one, and my rating for this will probably stay at 4.


Gameplay is smooth and moves at a brisk pace, but the core of the game is merely a get-cubes-to-get-more-cubes-to-get-VPs kind of game, a genre of game that I'm tired of.

In this game, the most interesting cards come from each faction's personal deck. These cards tend to be the most powerful in the game. However, in one game, a limited number come into play. So the game revolves around trying to get most of these cards built. I wished that all the cards in the game were as interesting as these faction cards -- most of the cards merely enable a player to get resources and/or VP at a somewhat better exchange rate.

RATING: 8
Pros:
1. Believe the Unbelievable. After 1 round of this game, you think to yourself, there's no way I can get anything going. By round 5, you're dealing with this massive tableau. It's great watching your tableau grow.
2. Asymmetry. Every faction having their own deck keeps the game interesting.
3. Fun cards/artwork. The artwork is fun and the actions available on the cards are interesting.

Cons.
1. Not balanced. Some factions score more points than other factions no matter how well you play. 
2. Player Interaction. Player interaction is extremely limited.
3. Bored. I loved this game at first. Then played a few more times... then a few more... then just lost interest in it. 

Summary: A solid tableau builder. I enjoy this game enough that I won't get rid of it, but not so much that I break it out often. A good game to break out with new gamers to give them a taste of tableau builders. The solo experience is solid, but also gets boring after playing each of the factions a few times.

RATING: 9.5
The art in this game, as Zee Garcia said, looks like a Facebook app. The art is corny and the theme is not my favorite, but the iconography/wording on the cards is certainly functional. Even with theme and art being important to me and missing the mark in Imperial Settlers, this game is in my top five games. The game play is brilliant and eloquent. Every time you think you are out of actions on a turn, you find another way to get more done. The back and forth of turns keeps you constantly engaged and taking actions. I have played one 4 player game, and I felt like it lingered a bit to long. I don't think I would play this one with more than 3, and that is only if everyone knew how to play, and played relatively quickly. I prefer two players. Once you start to learn how to make each faction do what they do, this game gets even better. ***Wife:9
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A streamlined version of "51. State" with accessible theme. The post-apocalyptic setting of the predecessor was gloomy enough to scare off a lot of people (females especially). "Imperial Settlers" design & art won hearts of many ladies - my wife's included.

Is it a better game overall? Well, that's debatale. "51. State" is a gamer's game - more complex, with many diverse strategies. "Imperial Settlers", while being very similar, is easier to play. Faction decks do a good job in focusing your efforts (barbarians are good in conquering, romans in building etc.) But that doesn't mean the younger brother is mindless - no, it's still a very thinky game! And if you didn't play "51. State" - both of the games are engine-builders in which you try to set up a good production of resources early to  score lots of points later. Turns are fast, one action and next player is ready to go. Still, it can drag a bit, especially with full player count. Also, the number of turns is not limited - if you aren't doing well, you will be forced to pass early and than watch as everybody else play for what seems like eternity. But fear not! "Imperial Settlers" is a game that you need to learn and with time you will do better. 

To sum it up: the art's amazing, the mechanics simple, but the gameplay demanding. Learning new faction decks is a joy in itself, because all of them play differently. Flaws? There is some luck involved (card game, doh!) and watching everybody else play and do well while your empire's in ruins is not very fun - but can sometimes happen. Other than that, solid game!

EDIT: Sold. Played the base game a lot and the expansions unfortunately unbalanced the game - much higher scores, players not using cards from the expansions are are bound to lose.

RATING: 7
[b]RO:[/b] [i]10/17/2015[/i]

You know? I really like Imperial Settlers. It's a very interesting, asymmetrical tableau building game. You've got your people (race, nation, ethnic group, sexual orientation (okay, not really), or whatever) and that has a deck and then you've got a common pool of cards everyone can draw from. You place cards which lets you get more resources (or more cards) and try to change those resources into other resources, cards, or victory points. It has conflict; you try to burn down your opponent's stuff while trying to stop him from burning down your stuff. It's not as big on direct conflict as I might be making it sound, it's really not, but it's there and an especially aggressive player might be able to really mess up your day. Also, this is another of those snowballing games, that is, you won't be doing much on the first turn but by the fourth turn the AP sets in and you've got so many disparate things going and so many actions to remember (and in what order to do them!) that it can be easy to get overwhelmed, specially' for new players. It's great fun though.

RATING: 5
One of my favourite engine builing game. You can do 2-3 different things with every card, some choices are very hard when you have to pick just one way to use it. 

The game feels very solitaire like. It varies between different factions and games, sometimes destroying important building may swing the game but it may also be the only time you interacted with the other player. You get more interactions with more players but the game drags a lot with every additional one. It got to a point i consider it unplayable with 4 players. 

If you got any expansions deck building is a must. Without it game is just super random. It takes few additional minutes but after one time you may use it again if you are happy with the effect.

I don't mind solitaire feel since me and my better half like these kind of games, since we play it with 2 bad draging with more players is not a problem.

RATING: 6
Imperial Settlers is not a terrible game, but it is hardly inspiring as a multiplayer game.

Ultimately, a game of IS is nothing more than a long monotonous sequence of micro-conversions of resources into VP with no rewarding feeling of exponential efficiency... sure you get more resources to play with after a couple of rounds, and some cards bounce off each others but all of this is broken down into additional micro-actions, only adding to the procedural development of the game.

Solo is where Imperial Settlers shines, a little brighter at least, allowing combos to be uninterrupted by opponents turns, the AI pretty much razing your buildings as randomly as other players would (at least for factions other than Japanese).

Rating as per multiplayer experience however, as I expect most people buying to primarily be interested in games with 2-4.

RATING: 6
when i was single, this was my favorite solitaire game. hands down. i thought the randomness would put me off, but somehow the game snowballs with a few extra card draws and ends up burning through most of your deck. i don't think that i've played this multiplayer since around the first month i bought it since it can draaag a little (or a lot) as people think. also, there's incentive to use swords or lose them which makes the take-that seem a little less strategic and more "sorry, don't hold it against me!" or random in the middle multiplayer rounds. 51st State re-imagined this sort of collect-build-destroy-replace cycle, streamlined it, took away the sting, and upped the theme ante. it's hard not to think that 51st State didn't replace Imperial Settlers for most people, and then Alien Artifacts replacing it in turn. beloved and underplayed though it is, i think i might purge this game in lieu of other resource snowball games like the Valeria series (with Mico art!!). now that i'm no longer single i haven't got the time to sit around and test my efficiency, so there actually might be a good deal more purging than replacing, however much i fondly remember these games and expansions.

RATING: 8
08-12-2014. Rated after 1 play. Imperial Settlers has a CIV theme, but basically is a cardgame/ resource engine building game. In the game you take control of one of 4 factions (romans, barbarians, japanese and egyptians) and try to build as many cards as you can from either your faction deck (worth VPs, cool special abilties) or the general deck.

Each round you draft some cards, draw some cards, get resources from played cards and your faction abilities and play new cards. Each time you take an action, your opponent gets his action directly afterwards. So while turns can be longish, the game does not feel too solitarish. Buildings can also be razed, by yourself or opponents and are worth resources then.

Fun game, highly recommended. Wanted to own this game. Only downside is the huge box. This box could AND SHOULD have been A LOT SMALLER IGNACY.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This one is an engine builder through and through and while the theme isn't glowing, I like the cute artwork.

Early game, it really hurts to give up some of your cards since they have such potential...but you know what? You need that wood NOW or you are going to get behind.  Those are fun decisions.  I also like that you can't keep goods between rounds so you are always optimizing for what you have on hand rather than 10 turns down the road (though you get a few of those decisions as well).

The only negatives would be that you can get a bit card screwed if few production buildings get drawn.  If that happens the game may drag as you pass and everyone else has 30 things to do.  This is fairly rare though and most of our games have been close even when someone THOUGHT there was a clear winner.

I still need to explore this one more though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Nice! I like the choices. Packed full of choices like which card to build and which to toss, and how. It's all very open. Mostly you'll go with production buildings to start with, but certainly not exclusively. A lot of the game is about seeing the path of resource acquisition and subsequent building acquisition.

The theme and simplicity of the card mechanic makes the game super easy to understand, learn and teach. One quick read of the excellent rulebook and you're away. With four different civilisations and so many cards you can get a decent number of plays out of this. Might be worth picking up the expansion sometime soon though.

The text is more often than not a lot smaller than it needs to be. Nothing I needed to bring out the microscope for (I'm thinking of one or two cards out of A Study in Emerald), but it would have been nicer if different not sizes were used and all the card real estate utilised. The contents of the box also needed a bit of an airing - the chemical smell is quite strong at first. Other than that the production quality is top notch.

The only issue I have with the game is that the last round can sometimes take an awfully long time. You'll have built up some decent production buildings and deals by this time. Combine that with current action cards and new buildings you'll build In the final round and the fifth round runs a little long with three or four players especially.

------------

With further play the game has bumped up another notch in the ratings. Speed of play has increased. Players are enjoying toying with the different factions and are hoping for some new ones in the future. Still some concern about the luck of the card draw, but if you don't get something good then you get something else good. It's all fun.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I go back and forth with Imperial Settlers. The solo game is a fun way to learn the factions, but does not stand up to repeated play. Roughly half of the action locations do very little or don't work at all against the dummy player, the way the dummy player attacks discourages keeping common locations out for extended periods, and beating the dummy player is trivial.

The basic mechanics of Imperial Settlers are pretty good, although I think I would like the game better if it was a little less possible to combo chain into doing more and more stuff, and if it had a turn structure where you took multiple actions on a turn in order to speed up the game. Once I've planned out my next 10 moves, I would like an opportunity to execute it without having to wait for every step individually. In addition, the base game faction deck distributions were a bit too inconsistent. Some of the cards weren't very useful, and if you didn't draw the production you needed to get started in the first round, you were pretty far behind. 

I'm also not the biggest fan of some of the player interaction in the game. The rest of the game is about building your economic engine, but the way some of the more hostile actions work can be a little frustrating. I think the raze tokens work well on the whole, but I'm not always satisfied with the way the other attack options work out in practice.

The expansions help a lot with the card distribution issue, and allow you to make the faction decks more reliable. The addition of open production buildings also helps mitigate getting a start without the production you need. You want to avoid using open production if you can generate the goods you need yourself, since it also helps your opponent, but it allows you to get a lot more done without production of your own in case of a rough start.

Overall I enjoy Imperial Settlers when playing with people that play fast, but I think the expansions are kind of necessary.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Imperial Settlers treads down the familiar path of card-driven, engine-building games. What's somewhat novel here are the attempts to balance out elements in the game; there is some destructive interaction in terms of razing opponent's buildings, but attacked players are able to turn razed locations into a foundation for bigger things. Also, players are able to make deals to compensate for a lack of resource cards early on. 

My main beef with Settlers is that the last two rounds often [i]feel[/i] stretched because players are engaged in a gazillion micro-conversions as they wring their engine dry. This is further worsened if the cards have simply stacked in favour of a particular player, and you can only watch on as he steamrolls his way to victory. The tiny text fonts also make it frustrating to read the cards in your opponent's tableau. As it stands, the current rating is for 2P games in which the listed drawbacks are mitigated; the rating steadily decreases with more players on board.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[2015-07-18]
Az 51st State játékot gondolta újra a szerző és célja annak javítása, eladhatóvá tétele volt. Nos, ez véleményem szerint tökéletesen sikerült. A történelmi, valós népek és gyönyörű grafika sokkal barátibb, mint a posztapokaliptikus sötétség. A játék hasonlóan zajlik, de mégis valahogy letisztultabb, egyszerűbb. Van egy saját és van egy közös paklink, amiből a kör elején lapot kapunk, majd termelünk és kezünkben levő lapjaink leépítésével fejlesztjük a birodalmunkat. A lapok ugyanúgy háromfunkciósak. Rombolhatók, körönként kinyerhetők belőlük termékek és használhatjuk a tulajdonságaikat is. A játék két fővel tökéletesen működött, egyértelműnek látszik, hogy ez az optimális játékosszám. 
Nézzük a felmerülő negatívumokat. 

Apró betűk a kártyákon. Tényleg kicsik, de szerintem tökéletesen láthatók. 

Hosszúság. Az 51-es szerintem hosszabb, elsőre nekünk is hosszú volt, de pár parti után, mikor minden kártya ismerős lesz, szerintem abszolút elfogadható a játékidő. Az utolsó kör nagyon hosszúra nyúlhat, számomra felvetődött az egy körrel rövidebb játék ötlete, de lehet úgy kiegyenlítetlen marad, illetve nem tudunk igazán kiteljesedni. 

Egyik nép erősebb, mint a másik. Itt érzek némi igazságot. Két játék alapján nekünk az egyiptomi tűnik erősnek és a japán kicsit gyengébbnek, de mindegyik játszható és mindennek van ellenszere, tehát nem is annyira a népek kiegyenlítetlensége a negatívum, hanem az a szerencsefaktor, ami a kiegyenlítetlenséget ellensúlyozná, azaz, hogy a megfelelő lap kijöjjön az erős lap ellenében. Két játék alatt kb. a saját paklink fele fogyott csak el, pedig próbáltam abból húzni, ha erre lehetőségem volt, mégse jött ki minden fajta lap. Ezt még további partik fogják eldönteni mennyire van erre ráhatásom és mennyi a szerencsefaktora a játéknak. De ha csak minden tizedik parti végződik nagy különbségű végeredménnyel, az szerintem elfogadható.

Összességében első játékra nekem két személlyel tökéletes volt. Két nagyon szoros partit játszottam, amit végig élveztem. Szóval én nem tudok belekötni igazán. Nagy újrajátszhatóságot érzek már az alapjátékban is. Nagyon erős 7-es. Ha hárman és négyen is ki tudom próbálni, illetve a szerencsét tudom még tesztelni mindenképpen nőhet a pont.

[2015-08-08]
Újabb két parti után, ahol sikerült kipróbálni a másik két fajt némileg romlott a véleményem a játékról. Egyrészt a japánok tényleg gyengébbek, mint a többiek, a másik három kb. hasonlóan erős. A véleményromlás két dologból fakad. Egyrészt a szerencse. Két hasonló képességű játékos partiját egyértelműen az fogja eldönteni. Nekem elég logikus minden lépés, adja magát melyik kártyát kell leépíteni, használni vagy szerződést kötni vele. Tehát kicsit mechanikussá válik. A másik dolog pedig a játék hossza. Korábban azt írtam nem vészes, de most újabb két parti egyenként másfél óráig tartott, ami kicsit azért hosszú egy kétszemélyes viszonylag könnyed játék esetén. 1 óra elég lenne egy parti esetén. A játék nem rossz, a pontján se rontok még. Szeretném többen is kipróbálni, utána mondok majd végső verdiktet.

RATING: 10
Imperial Settlers is exactly my kind of game. It takes several of my favorite mechanisms, chiefly multi-use cards, engine building, and asymmetrical play, and combines them into a deep and highly re-playable game. While each faction is different, how you play is not. So rather than having to relearn the game for each faction, you simply have to discover the nuances that they add. It's simple to learn, but difficult to master. However you choose to approach it, it's a very enjoyable game and one of my all-time favorites.

The game lasted much longer than I expected (2 hours+), but that is probably because it was the first game for all 3 of us. Definitely need to play again with another faction but the overall first experience wasn't that much fun.

RATING: 3
There's some take-that interaction, but Imperial Settlers is a mostly solitaire engine-building card game where players get goods, convert them into other goods, and pump out victory points. Most of the VP generation occurs around the mid to late game. General strategies are baked into the four faction decks, and successful execution depends on the cards players happen to draw in the early to mid game. Some cards are a lot more useful than others, and it's entirely possible that you'll never see your most useful cards, or you'll see them too late in the game for them to make a difference. In any case, it's highly unlikely that I'll ever see them. I'm just not interested in this sort of experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
1 play, 3er

Players each have their own, nation-specific deck. In addition, there's a common deck of cards. There are 5 rounds in the game, each with the same sequence:
1. Draw one card from your own deck, then draft 2 cards from the common deck.
2. Produce resources based on your race and the cards in your tableau
3. In turn order, players use their resources and cards in their display to take a single action, continuing until everyone passes. Actions allow you to draw more cards, play cards into your display, activate cards (for resources, cards, VP, etc.), convert resources, etc.
4. Cleanup: discard all your remaining resources, except whatever your nation can store.

Each nationality plays differently, with cards that give everyone different abilities. The common cards can be built into your display, like the nation cards, but can also be raided to get more resources. You can also raze other players' common cards (and the Japanese player's cards) for resources. If a player's card is raided, as compensation they get one wood (resource). Also, razed cards are not totally useless: they flip upside down to become foundations, which can be used the help play other cards. Still, you do lose the card's special ability! Fortunately, there's not necessarily a lot of raiding -- the required swords are not so plentiful!

Wow, cool game! It doesn't seem particularly balanced, but that could just be inexperience talking. Or maybe the Egyptians are just too easy to play / strong? I definitely want to play this again!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
SOLO Core Game
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
1-4, 45-90m
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I love variety and variability in my games and for that reason I really like this game in what it provides to players and how it plays.  However, there are certainly some balancing issues that keep me from loving it.

The game is a light pseudo-civilization building game.  Each player picks a faction which comes with it's own deck of cards as well as slightly different production and a different resource they can store (all other resources not spent are lost between each of the game's 5 rounds).  There is also a common deck of cards and each of these cards shows a different building that can be built and added to your civilization.  These buildings either produce a resource every round, provide a new action that can be taken during future turns usually at the cost of resources, or give your civilization a passive ability.

I call it pseudo-civilization building because what it really is is a much simpler tableau building game.  The game even at max players can play in an hour, is completely card driven and the card play is 99% of the game.  There is no other combat mechanism or technology tree.

While the decks aren't super varied they provide enough options and the asynchronous player factions/decks offer enough more variety that I am constantly interested and able to pursue different paths to victory with each play.  There's also a decent amount of player interaction, if you want there to be.  You can certainly play this game with a much more hermit-like mindset worrying just about advancing your own civilization and ignoring your opponents, hopefully they ignore you back.  All of this keeps me very engaged.  However asynchronous games absolutely NEED balance to work and this game doesn't have it.

The Egyptian faction is far and away better than the other three.  A new player playing this faction can beat even experienced players playing another pretty regularly.  The Egyptians in the hands of an experienced player will probably win 99 times out of 100.  Every time something like this is brought up you are going to see just as many or more people claiming no, the Egyptians are not overpowered.  They're wrong, they're just fanboys that can't stand to have criticism brought against a game they like.  The Egyptians make the game no fun for anybody.  Sure, you could just not play with this faction but then you are left with just 3 options and no way to play the full complement of 4.  This faction legitimately hurts the game.

In fact, in general the one thing I wish they did was put more factions in the game.  They announce unashamedly in the rulebook that expansions are in the works and not only will we get new factions but the ability to build our own faction decks by mixing cards from all the factions.  Until then though even with the varied different decks and playstyles playing the same 4 will get old and nobody wants to play with the Egyptians in the game.  I don't know how this faction got through the playtesting process with the pedigree of the designer behind it.

RATING: 8
My rating for solo play. Will probably rate higher after more plays. Fantastic medium weight solo game. Variety of factions and action cards keeps this game interesting every time. RATING: 6
I enjoy this game despite its flaws.  I really like asymmetrical play in games, and this one does a good job at faction balance, in my opinion.  The mechanics are straightforward, and there's just a bit of "take that," without making being on the losing end of the affair too terribly punishing.  My main complaint is that this game suffers from what many card games suffer from; a substantial luck component.  If you just happen to get lucky and get the faction cards that will get your engine going quickly, you can make an early run and get a lead that's difficult to overcome.  Nonetheless, all of our games have played out fairly closely.

RATING: 6.9
When [thing=73369][/thing] was explained to me as [thing=28143][/thing] but better, I got pretty exited, as that is one of my top 2 ever games. But after playing it twice, my review is more like: somewhat like RFTG but not nearly as good. They're both card games in which you build a tableau, but other than that they don't have too much in common.
I remember feeling that something didn't sit right with the game, but I don't remember what that could have been.
Imperial Settlers is a rethemed version of 51st State. Or so the splainer told me. That could very well be as some of the elements sounded familiar indeed, but it's been a couple of years since I played 51st State. But whatever didn't sit right with that game, didn't bother me this time around.
The mechanisms work fine and the game goes along at a very pleasant pace. Everyone has a different faction with a different starting set, which should add to the replay value. One thing that annoyed me slightly is that all of us only used a small part of their deck. I used every chance I had to draw cards from my deck, yet I only went through about a third of the deck. If the card's you are planning for are in the other part of the deck, you're out of luck.
A very enjoyable game, but I'm not sure yet about how good it actually is.

RATING: 4
Combo-building card game in the guise of a civilization builder. Learning the strategy for each faction will be key. I've played solo and 2-player. The turns are quick, which is a positive that minimizes downtime, but this is really a game that is about optimizing lots of small interactions. I'm worried that it's all just too much chrome for what is essentially a straightforward concept accomplished equally well by other, shorter and smaller card games.

And a note about the components. The artwork is cute and creative, but hardly fitting for a civ-type game about invasion. Moreover, the icons and text are tiny -- and I mean minuscule -- which is really too bad.

RATING: 7
Really just a top flight Engine Building game... it doesn't quite match to depth and complexity of a title like Terraforming Mars, but it also manages to be at least a little bit more approachable for casuals, and perhaps more importantly, runs faster and involves less cleanup/teardown. Also unlike Terraforming Mars (and many games of all stripes claiming to have one), it's Solo Variant is quite pleasing. My biggest issue is as someone who hates "Gotcha"/"Take That" mechanics, the the Razing mechanic is a bit too focal in play... there's a pacifist variant in the core guide that restricts this component I highly recommend if you're in a similar boat, but there's no wheedling it out completely... While I'm under the impression 1 or 2 may be out already, I'll definitely say it could benefit from some expansions adding factions... while not unbalanced per say, they four base civs all play different enough that it can feel like you're all using the same score system when playing differnt games. I'll also say that despite being pretty simple, teaching yourself to play from the guide or a tutorial video... murky wording and pacing doesn't help. If you know someone who has played before, try to get them to teach you

RATING: 10
This game just hits a sweet spot for me. I love civilization building, this has it. I love resource management, this has it. I love card drafting, this has it. The theme is perfect for me and the artwork and graphic design make me so happy every time I pick up the box. The feel of the game is lighthearted but the depth will keep you coming back for more. Each civilization that you play will feel different and require you to make different decisions. There is a deck building aspect as well that creates tons of variability and replay value. Imperial Settlers also has the best solo variant of any tabletop game I have ever played. The solo mode keeps the same feel of the game play and allows you to perfect the strategies of each civilization without having to round up a group to play. There is an "AI" deck so to speak included with the game and separate rules for the incredible solo version of the game. There is also a wonderful free campaign mode released online by this game's incredible designer Ignacy Trzewiczek that you can download and print to create even more depth and replay value to the solo mode. This game is a masterpiece in my opinion and one that I will never get rid of. I believe in this game and I believe in this company. Portal games makes quality products and because they are not a major corporation with hundreds of employees they take personal care and attention to the details of their games and the passion shows. So if you have a chance, go out and buy Imperial Settlers. I don't think you will regret it.
