=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
Imperial Settlers waddles up to the table sporting an adorable cover. There's a smiling puppy on the cover! Everything seems so cute... until you start to play. As you listen to the rules explanation and how an opponent can raze your buildings to the ground, you start to realize that this might not be a cute and adorable game after all.

This is a game about building cards, to get stuff, to build more cards, to score points. Or... erm... you're a civilization trying to build the best civilization. Or something. But there's cards that do cool things that let you combo on other cards with all sorts of neat interactions.

I remember starting the game and thinking that five turns didn't seem like enough time to do anything in the game. It didn't really seem like there was much you could do on your turn... but as it turns out there are a lot of ways that you can build buildings that let you open up new opportunities on your turn and turns start to spiral out with everything you can do.

Some cards give you stuff every turn. Some cards let you trigger off things you've already built. Some cards let you do cool actions. Communal cards don't give you as many points as your personal faction cards, and your personal cards have civilization specific abilities, some of which are pretty cool. Cards can be built, but some of them can be turned into "deals" (a free thing every turn), or "razed" (sacrificed for a one-time payout of something).

To raze a card in your hand requires a sword token... but you can spend two sword tokens to raze an opponent's already built building. But it's not all frowns for the other guy as he gets a wood, and his razed building can be built over. As most of the building require you to build over an existing building anyway, that's not so bad. Of course, if it's a building you really needed it can be painful.

Apparently there are concerns about game balance. First, because there are a lot of cards and you may never see the "good" cards in your deck. And second in that some civilizations are just better than others. It's hard to have an opinion on that after a single play.

Egypt did seem pretty powerful, but the Barbarians finished right behind them. And while Rome didn't do so well in this game, the player who won with Egypt won with Rome in his previous play. Japan didn't seem to fare so well, but I wasn't really paying attention to what other people were doing very much, so I don't really know why that was. To be fair, Rome nixed a building that would have score Japan a lot of points, so that did seem a little unbalanced, but I'm not sure that it really changed the outcome very much.

I was worried at the outset that the "take that"-ness of razing buildings would be painful, but it only happened a few times and didn't really seem to put anyone out too much. The destroy a building power of Rome was a fear until I realized that I didn't have a particular building that really was key to my plans... something that probably bothered Japan a bit more. My one real frustration with the game is that it probably is a good idea to know what other people are doing, and it's hard enough to track everything you're doing much less anyone else.

I really enjoyed this game. Trying to figure out the cards and puzzle out a plan to make everything come together was a lot of fun. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game definitely lives up to its Hype!!  It is just fun to play.  

I do agree about the name of the game (not great), and some of the print although very easy and intuitive, it is too small to see across the table, and you definitely need to be aware and know what buildings you need to "raze".... (Eliminate) from your opponents empire....!!  You also need multiple plays, to really start knowing the combos and what to look for.

In the end this game will get smothered with expansions, and have great replay value, but then will be too much I think, although a few new Factions will be awesome.   

Buy it, and play it, very intuitive, and a lot of fun.  

Would be a 9, but can be a slight bit finicky, and the size of the print and symbols on the cards is too small.

But again, buy it, it is a great game!!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
After ~25 plays, I'm still trying to figure this one out (mostly played 2 player, no expansions).  I don't mean the strategies, but rather what I like and dislike about this game.  In some ways, the game plays plays simply and beautifully.. and I'm hooked.  In other ways, it just feels like something is off.  Something is missing or not fully tested. 

My first 10 plays or so were with the general rules for the "Lookout Phase" (card drafting).  I recommend ignoring those and just diving in to the advanced rules.. this lets you base your success somewhat more on your strategy and less on luck of the draw. 

But still, some games everything can be running smoothly, and other games not at all.. and it's often just because you can't seem to get a single building that doesn't require a foundation (or other building) cost.... or no production whatsoever... or something similar.  These games aren't often, but when they happen they can be frustrating. 

Pros:
+Fun gameplay
+Love asymmetric "civ" game (though I use civ loosely)
+Some "take that" but it's not too strong nor even necessary in many cases to be effective
+Great artwork.  Unlike many, I LOVE the box art.. but I also love the card art. 
+Well balanced, overall

Cons:
-Some concerns about consistency of balancedness due to poor card draws.  Maybe this will be fixed with more plays... but I've just seen some starting hands that.. *shiver*  It's difficult to catch up.

Update: 7/12/2017
Really getting the hang of deals early on to make the best of no production card draws, as well as using workers to draw new cards.  The combination of these techniques has increased my love for the game 10-fold. Gets better every time.

Update 10/19/2017
Game continues to improve.  Solid game; give it a chance and let it grow.  You have to get familiar with the mechanics, especially Deals, to get things moving along well.

RATING: 7.5
Imperial Settlers is a solid, fun, tactical resource/hand management game for 2 or 3 players. Each round is a tactical puzzle, building more cards and shifting around resources, in which you try to use all options open to you the best you can. And there can be quite a lot of options near the end of the game! I really like the (very) different faction decks and the interaction between them. But be aware - since you only get to draw part of your faction deck each game, the speed at which points are earned can be rather swingy. Just handsomely won a game through a card that I drew in my last turn.

RATING: 8
Imperial Settlers is my favorite euro style game and deftly combines excellent card drafting and worker placement mechanics with asymmetric faction abilities, and varied and interesting player interaction mechanics. It is in an excellent looking game which provides for a thoroughly engaging experience at all player counts. 

Components: The artwork on the cards is really vibrant and beautiful. It really draws you to the game and encourages you to stay at the table, if only to see what all the different cards look like. The resources are represented by tokens that come in relevant shapes. The faction boards are also well illustrated and functional. 

Ease of learning/teaching: The core flow and mechanics of the game are fairly straightforward. The 3 different types of cards can be explained quickly, with the bulk of the mechanics explained on the cards themselves. A lot of questions crop up during play regarding building interactions which are not quite adequately explained in the rule book. However, a quick search through the BGG forums will usually solve that problem.

Theme: The game does justice to its theme. The different factions play differently, according to the historic proclivities of the corresponding empires. The different designs on the faction cards are lighthearted and fun. The style of interaction of each faction with other factions is also designed to play along the theme. 

Gameplay: The game is essentially about building a tableau of common and faction cards which are fueled by the resources that are produced in order to generate points. However, unlike other tableau builders where your engine is continually growing, in Imperial Settlers, your tableau evolves. One has to discard common locations in order to build most of the faction locations, so that what your engine produces or does is continually changing as you build new cards and moves you in a different direction.

Once you have your cards in hand, the game boils down to figuring out the correct sequence of actions so as to maximize the number of things you can do and the points you can generate in a turn. Player interaction ranges from destroying other factions' buildings to stealing their deals, or just appropriating usage rights for a turn. They are varied, tempered and never quite so rampant as to breed too much unnecessary hostility at the table. 

The game is somewhat intuitive in terms of tactical play but less intuitive when it comes to long term strategies. New players will feel hamstrung by inadequate production cards in early game and not focus on drawing enough cards. 

The solo variant of the game also provides a very engaging experience and was recently augmented with  campaign rules.

Imperial Settlers is one of those games where the whole is certainly greater than the sum of its parts. It always makes for a fulfilling time and gives you enough to think about without crippling you. I greatly look forward to fielding it every time.

RATING: 6
Imperial Settlers is a good game, but not one of our favorites.  It seems like a game where it's pretty easy to get lost in your own little world unless you're really focused on razing your opponents buildings.  The faction to pick is VERY important as this will determine what goods you can store between turns and what goods you'll be earning early in the game.  (The factions do make sense thematically, which is a nice touch.)  This does mean you might have a different strategy every time you play, but also means you're more or less forced into a certain strategy.  Despite that, it doesn't feel extremely replayable.  You're basically just building buildings every game, hoping cards that benefit you come into your hand and trying to get the resources to build them once they do.  Not a bad game...but not one that jumps off the shelf onto the table on a regular basis.

RATING: 6.5
Definitely an adorably attractive game with slick gameplay.  I'm just feels like it's missing heart to me.  It's over so quick, and there's less interaction than I'd like.  Feel-wise, Bruges gives you a similar sort of, "here's your hand for this round, make it work" feel, and setting up long-term combo's, but the push your luck elements and competing for goals brings it together more and adds some excitement that I feel is lacking in Imperial Settlers.

I like confrontation but with no real way to prevent it aside from picking one immune building each turn, it was really frustrating in this game. So it's a largely solo experience where people can throw monkey-wrenches in your engine. Eh...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Luck-based production engine building euro. Very streamlined gameplay that doesn't require player aids or constant rulebook lookups. First game was a good game, drew cards that gave me a chance to focus my play and gain develop. Second game that was likely my last game was ruined by bad card draws during the first three rounds. The game suffers from random draws very much even if we played with a lookout phase draft variant. For example if you never draw any cards that allow you to gain more cards and at the same time never draw any more worker production cards your game will inevitably stall and there's nothing to do about it, recovering later in the game is too late as the engine base needs to be laid in the first three rounds. Couple this with another player who is doing well and can raze any new good building you build and we have a game that I will never touch again, even if it would sometimes work out well. House ruling away the randomness is an option but a game should not need house rules to be playable.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Excellent game, I've barely tried New Era but definitely see the descendance, Imperial Settlers is a very streamlined game,it flows beautifully and there are almost no rule exceptions/starnge cases (contrary to Robinson Crusoe ^^). I would not class it as a familly game though, it's easy to pick and play but hard to perform well in it.

[+] like the art, great components, love the faction decks, streamlined gameplay, lots of options, seems well balanced
[-] a single card can be huge if you can get it (the red scoring for japanese, the black one for barbarian, etc.); why such a big box :(, takes a lot of table space

realy want/need to try:
- expansion: Done
- deck building: Done
- fan expansion
- solo mode: Done
- solo campaign: Done

2015 will see a lot of IS plays ^^

UP: solo campaign: I tried it with all factions, it works really well (fast paced, short game, lots of choices), but as it is now, it is really easy. The virtual opponent is no threat at all, the border faction taht provides a few cheap deals is a huge boost and the achievements are too cheap for the amazing bonus they provide. The only challenge comes from the provinces with increasing upkeep (resources each round) and control cost (-VP each game), but at least for 5-8 games, the achievements makes up for them and it was never challenging to win (most of the time scoring in the 80-100 range). Probably need some tweeks to be more challenging (in my opinion a good solo game should be a struggle to make you want to come back and beat it)

UP: after playing IS a lot (and it seems getting good at it), the game tends to get way longer when you know what your doing. maybe also because of the expansions? because good players make good decisions in early rounds, there is a snowball effect, where the rounds keep getting longer and longer. Focusing on card draw has been a best strategy to maximize points, but also increase play time. I've seen games where all faction buildings have been drawn during round 4. For most factions, the best strategies are to draw cards and wait until a combo can be played. All in all, I still like the game but I don't get it played any more because I know it will be very long... it's curious, usually getting better at a game decreases playtime, IS is the opposite. (+ there is no fun in playing against someone new to the game, who will be crushed by an experienced player)

gone in MathTrade Jul16

RATING: 7
Wasn't expecting too much from what looked like a generic euro VP gaining engine clone, but this title has more character, more replayability, and more interaction than it appears at first blush.  The faction differentiation is a nice touch, and the ability and necessity to raze other players' buildings adds a significant bite that is missing from a lot of Eurogames.  Imperial Settlers is a pretty dumb name, and the whimsical art sometimes rings a bit false, but I'm quite happy to play this anyway.

RATING: 10
Update 2017: Maybe not my most favorite game of all time, but it's among them. It holds up to multiple plays, but not quite as well as I was hoping. 

The feeling of building up for the first turn or two, then stretching your limited resources as far as they'll go, then finishing the last turn or two with a ton of resources and trying to squeeze every last VP out your civ as possible is exactly what I'm looking for in a resource management board game. 

After playing expansion 1, I love it! Open Production is an awesome addition and added a bit more player interaction as well. Atlanteans is next up... not sure how to feel about that radically different faction!
Update 2017: Atlanteans actually integrated a lot better than I expected them to! Good addition!

Update Q4 2017: Oh no. 51st State Master Set showed up and it MIGHT bump this off my best games of all time. Hmmm, time will tell.

RATING: 7
This is supposed to be a more streamlined version of 51st state. I'm sorry to see the original post-apocalyptic theme let go in favor of an ancient civilization skin, but it's not a deal breaker. I'm still not sure how I feel about the art direction, whether it's cute and whimsical or annoying. Mostly because it looks like something you'd see in a banner ad.

The game play is smooth. Draft a couple cards, activate some cards and build what you can. Between the community deck and the individual civilization decks, there are a lot of cool interactions that make every turn a new puzzle to work out. It's a nice marriage of drafting and CCG/TCG style combos.

RATING: N/A
Interactive civ builder. Barbarians, Egyptians, Japanese and Romans race to build or raze locations (yours or theirs), make deals, or play actions. Most cards are played to your own player board from your unique faction deck or from drafted common cards.  The key is to take advantage of the unique strength of your faction deck. Build resource and power upgrades while fending off attacks from opponents. Pig pile on runaway leaders. Some factions are more aggressive and some have strengths that are more subtle.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I've played Imperial Settlers a number of times now and it has become more enjoyable with every play. It is important that you play up to the strengths of your particular faction as using the same strategy for one group won't necessarily work for another. This is where multiple plays becomes important as, not only do you gain a better understanding of how your own faction plays and when it's the most opportune time to play certain cards, but you also have a better understanding about how your opponents play and what you need to do to thwart their plans.

Don't let the 'cute' graphics fool you, there's quite a bit of interaction in the game, which is good... and in most cases... necessary.

I also really enjoy how the cards can be used in multiple ways. Do you want to build it for its ability? Can you not afford it and instead want to turn it in to a deal? Is it better for you to raze the card from your hand so that you can use the resources to undertake a better move on your next turn? Is everything that I just struggled to do about to get destroyed by my opponent?

While I've played this with all player counts, and I think that it works well with them all, I think that it really shines as a three player game. From what I've heard, the first expansion also seems to really add more friendly interaction in the game as the only interaction in the based game was generally destroying your opponents building.

And speaking of expansions... the game is designed for expansions and I'm already interested in picking them up as the come out. Given the mechanically sound nature of the game and its seemingly endless expand-ability options, I don't think that I'll grow tired of this game any time soon.

-- UPDATE --

While my comments above (2015) seem to love the idea of expansions, my current self has actually cooled on the game due to expansions. What was an easy to pick up game has all now become something that requires deck-building and additional time commitments. This is not what I had originally hoped for. (See comment on Atlantean's expansion)

RATING: 8.4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.7
[after 1 game (1@1p)]
Got the gist of the game by playing solo Romans, and I might had a glimpse of why people said the game is unbalanced. Steamrolled the game, got VERY close to the 80+ point threshold.
I've found it an interesting resource management exercise, promising game.


[after 2 games (1@4p,1@1p)]
First multiplayer game with full rig of opponents, all new players. I try to avoid picking the Romans, the others insist on not choosing them (especially when I tell them about their reputation, I guess nobody wants to incur in the risk of being "the one who won just because of an unfair advantage"), so I end up playing them again.
Got two small rules wrong that probably didn't have a huge impact (i.e. the 1 wood compensation for being razed and the possibility of spending more resource to activate one action multiple times in a turn).
Romans steam-rolled the opponents after a very slow point start. By the looks of it, the Egyptian player played enough to the faction strengths, the Barbarian had some bad luck on his own faction cards (no production buildings at all until the last round), and the Japanese player - after an early lead in points - got simply stuck without being able to play on its strengths (had piles and piles of unused food and very few buildings).

I think that Romans really have it easy at this point. Once they manage to get out one or two of their buildings that reward them for building GREY and ROMAN buildings, they just start the engine at full throttle. Maybe I haven't seen the full picture, but they seem to be very very focused in what they do: produce stone, build point-generating buildings for faction and grey buildings, start building non-stop. Additional points come from action buildings that let you spend stone, and the additional raze tokens you can stack allows you to get extra points/resources and mess a bit of the opponents plans.
The only critical aspect is making sure that by the time you start the building chain you have the drawing capabilities to sustain it.
Problem is, you don't really depend on anybody or anything out-of-faction to accomplish this strategy, as most stuff you have it in-house. Except some early stone-generating buildings, the common cards you want are the ones which are cheaper to spend as foundations (and of course the ones that let you draw, which you should defend promptly).

I've read a very interesting and comprehensive piece by "PinkMeeple" about the game, and I do disagree with the statement that drawing cards is not the most important thing in this game, especially for the Romans, here's why:
- production cards usually pay for themselves (i.e. you get back what you spend right away), so... why not? Worst case you have a foundation to use on a better faction card right away... but you have to have the card in hand of course!
- common cards can be razed cheaply and gain you immediate resources: more often than not, I felt like I would have razed only my cards all day if only I had more in my hand, why spend more to get potentially the same and just give minor hassle to the opponent (remember that production buildings pay for themselves, so razing those usually is almost beneficial for the opponent unless it is a very valuable one);
- faction cards can be used to make deals, which generate resources cheaply and cannot be razed (although the Japanese can screw you on that), but you need to draw excess faction cards in order to have the "luxury" of using them as deals.

All in all, card draw is a very crucial way to enact production (raze and deals). Producing a lot of resources allows you to realise any strategy really, but you need - again - cards to make the best use out of them. At the beginning I'm more than happy to draw common production cards as they mostly pay for themselves and grant me at least an extra resource next round (or this one, if they get razed), if I happen to draw an action or feature card I'm not interested in, it's raze-fodder for more resources. The first two turns may be tough as you don't have the required flexibility and optimisation is tricky, but the more you go onward the more you become flexible... IF you have a strong card draw.

I want to play again with a different faction. My biggest fear right now is that most factions are one-dimensional enough to require expansions to keep the game any interesting.

[after 3 games (1@4p,1@3p,1@1p)]
Managed to play another game with 3, same group as last game but one less guy. This time we didn't take the Romans, but from my perspective the outcome was the same, and I think I actually got more points than in my last game playing the Japanese.
It has to be said that the girl playing the Barbarian did a couple of mistakes early on that completely ruined her game, and since the other guy was playing the Egyptians he was not really interested in messing up with me by razing etc.
I can see how in this base game each faction has sort of a couple of ways to do its thing, or at least a couple of aspects it excels at to increase its resource curve and then gather points.

All in all the Japanese were more fun to play compared to the Romans I think, and they have some really powerful special cards.
However I didn't substantially change my strategy in this game, as I have focused heavily on faction buildings. It just required different resources and different side-actions to increase the benefits of my strategy. Daimyo Castles helped quite a bit, and compared to the Romans I had many more cards that I struggled to use as deals because I really wanted to see built.

At the level of interaction that we've been floating around right now, I'd say that Imperial Settlers is very much a multiplayer solitaire, but a very good solitaire nonetheless.
Occasionally the fear of raze tokens does force you to consider your opponents, but on the other hand judging when the opponents are going to pass can affect your play if you are the one who wants to raze something.

I'm assessing a tentative "8" for now, but it has potential to fluctuate around that. Given my history with most games, the fourth game is kind of revealing: if it survives the fourth play with stable or increasing marks, then it's definitely a keeper and I'll probably consider buying expansions to increase variety.

[after 4 games (2@4p,1@3p,1@1p)]
Played a fourth game after a while, this time I used the Egyptian but still the same outcome: I won by a very large margin with more than 110 points. I guess I'm lucky and good enough at the game while the people I've been playing with are consistently worse... dunno.

In this game we had two new players and one who was at her third game (she came in last though, and with the Romans... the irony!).

I'm decreasing the score of this because the heaviness of the "solitaire" aspect for me, combined with the length of the game that we've been experiencing, is too much. I feel like I'm playing a 3 hours long Race for the Galaxy: I love race, but I love it also because it's meaty decisions and combos in a short amount of time.
Another game that I feel somewhat relates to this for me is another favorite of mine: Seasons. In my experience they are comparable in length, but the much higher level of interaction provided by Seasons and the very structure of the turn and timing elevate it to a much superior level compared to Imperial Settlers. One thing that I thought I could do without in Seasons is fiddlyness, but Imperial Settlers is even worse at this with all its beautiful yet numerous resource tokens that you are constantly spending and gaining.

Long story short: I feel two games that I have in my collection fill the same niche of this in a better way (Race being faster, Seasons being more tense and satisfying).
I still think it's a very nice game that will appeal to those who like low interaction (although to such people even the raze mechanism could be annoying) and don't like the space theme of race and the symbols.
Another game that I own that relates to this in my book is San Juan (makes sense since I compared this to Race): again, to me it feels very similar to Imperial Settlers with a simplified resource system.
I'm not trading it for now, but I'm not pursuing expansions. Would play if asked, but wouldn't probably propose it often myself.

[after 5 games (2@4p,2@3p,1@1p)]
Nothing new here, picked it up after a while and played the Barbarians (first time for me) against Romans and Egyptian: another win, although this was a bit closer than previous games.
I stand by my previous conclusions: in 3 it took us roughly 2 hours to finish a game. A bit too long for what it is, and I still find annoying the juggling of resources.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Man, this is such a cute little civilization builder. I've only played solo mode, which I fear is much different than the multiplayer I intend to play this weekend, but it's pretty solid. Five rounds makes it super short, and really forces you to make the best decisions and chain building abilities together. I've played against the AI twice: I lost the first one because I only half-read the rules and couldn't possibly win, and then I beat the pants off it.

RATING: 8
Jól összerakott játék, könnyen érthető (magyar) szabállyal, szép színes, jó grafikájú kártyákkal. A tablóépítés miatt nálam előnnyel indult, de aztán tényleg rászolgált a nevére. Többszöri játék után is szeretem, kifejezetten élvezetesen. Továbbra is csak két fővel játsszuk, szerintem így a legjobb élmény és a downtime sem vészes ennyien.
